```
On 12/1/2010 11:33 AM, RealInfo wrote:
> On Dec 1, 6:29 pm, Kerry Liles
> <"kerry.liles[minus_the_spam]"@gmail.com>  wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> EC

That depends on a lot of other factors: what mainframe? what kinds of 
file access: indexed files or databases (leading to what type of 
database); what type of terminal interaction etc. Some combinations of 
such factors cannot be debugged or run on a PC (at least not with a 
reasonable amount of work).

The first question you need to find an answer for is whether there is a 
compatible COBOL compiler for the PC for the mainframe environment you 
are targeting... OPENCOBOL might do the trick, or Fujitsu COBOL or MF 
COBOL, but so much depends on the mainframe capabilities you wish to 
emulate.
```

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-06T11:46:34+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8m2j2bFhb4U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9929b75f-a8a2-41e4-aa16-b274c41d5b30@r29g2000yqj.googlegroups.com> <baacde63-8a24-4f0a-b8f6-67a32071db8b@o4g2000yqd.googlegroups.com> <id6702$j7u$1@reader1.panix.com> <30bb2e97-5880-4822-aede-b5ed804fd9d9@b25g2000vbz.googlegroups.com> <id8gn8$lp6$1@reader1.panix.com> <57f2fc28-4f07-464c-83f5-955cb36c5dea@p11g2000vbn.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Dec 2, 4:15 pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
>> In article
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> constitute begging in some quarters.

Having read the thread, and not wishing to take sides, I did want to offer 
the following observations on this point from Alistair:

1. A person who works as a freelance is likely (and, in my opinion, has a 
perfect right to...) promote the position that if you want something done 
professionally, there are professional people available to do it. I don't 
think that constitutes "begging".

2. Being between contracts is normal for most contractors and does not imply 
a level of "desperation" to be re-employed. In a contract career which 
lasted from 1975 until 2005 I was out of work (when I would have preferred 
to be working) for a total of around 21 days. Although this mileage may vary 
for others, I believe it is fairly normal for competent contractors (and 
there is no question o f the Doc's competence) to take time out and to be a 
bit selective before diving back in to the stresses of the workplace. We all 
need some time out and contractors don't get paid holidays.

3. If a contractor WERE anxious to be employed, it is unlikely that frequent 
posts to this forum, reminding people that professionalism comes at a cost, 
would be a good way to obtain offers. I am therefore persuaded that this is 
simply the Doc's position on what should be paid for and what should be 
free, and has nothing to do with his current employment status.

4. I believe the REAL issue here is what can be given away for free and what 
should actually be paid for. Over years many people have contributed and 
received help through this forum. Sometimes even very capable people can use 
a different perspective and just discussing approaches to a problem can be 
helpful, sometimes there are people here with specific expertise in areas 
which are not generally covered in text books and manuals and they are 
prepared to generously give their time and advice. The question arises: 
Should people be paid for giving advice here?

My own feeling is: "No"  My reason for that opinion is that you have a 
perfect right to post here or not and it is an individual choice. No matter 
how capable you may be personally, there may come a time when you could use 
some help. Doc's stated position is that you should buy that help from a 
professional and I don't see that as being "wrong", but neither do I see it 
as being the only possible response. Rather, I see times when some free 
advice would be appropriate, even if simply indicating a direction for 
further exploration, or pointing out some of the less obvious implications 
of a particular solution, but I also see times when it would be appropriate 
to offer paid services. If the solution being requested is likely to 
generate revenue for the requestor, and requires information or skill which 
they patently don't have, then they should be prepared to pay something to 
obtain the solution. Similarly, if a provided solution can save someone a 
good deal of time, then they should recognise that value. For myself, I try 
to acknowledge where something has been helpful and will do this with money 
if the provided advice helps me to generate revenue. Just because there is a 
forum with some generous spirits in it doesn't mean we should take that for 
granted and EXPECT free advice.

(Sometimes, even in a commercial environment, it isn't about money. I 
recently had a client who got into a major project and simply ran out of 
money. PRIMA continued support and never turned off the tap because he 
couldn't pay us. We supported our tools, and provided COBOL expertise 
because we said we would, not because there was a maintenance contract in 
place (there wasn't; he had no money to pay for one...). Today this same 
client is a valued customer who pays his bills on the nail, extols our 
company and services to anyone who will listen, and hasn't forgotten that we 
supported him. I can't buy that kind of loyalty. The point is that there are 
times when you should do somethng for nothing and there are times when you 
shouldn't.)

I guess all of us can relate to seeing someone struggling with a programming 
problem and most of us would want to help (maybe based on the conscious or 
subconscious realization that we could be in the same position ourselves).

But there are always the freeloaders who are looking for a free ride, 
contribute nothing to the forum, and usually don't even say "thank you" when 
they get what they came for.

The trick is for all of us to recognize the difference,and that is a purely 
subjective call.

Pete.
```

### COBOL to PC , makes sense ?

- **From:** RealInfo <therightinfo@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-01T10:31:48-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<58e42b89-8a5b-4a2a-aaaf-e3a7c541575a@39g2000yqa.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi all

Does it make sense to develop COBOL applications
for the PC as final target ?

Do enterprise companies use/prefer COBOL apps that run on a PC
or a PC server, or the mainframes are here to dominate
the enterprise market ?

I ask it because I do not own a mainframe I have to develop COBOL apps
on a PC .

Thanks in advance
EC
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL to PC , makes sense ?

- **From:** Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@googlemail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-02T03:53:39-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0fb822b6-0d14-4f1c-9151-58e7a14a3209@k13g2000vbq.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 1, 6:31 pm, RealInfo <therighti...@gmail.com> wrote:
> Hi all
>
> Does it make sense to develop COBOL applications
> for the PC as final target ?

No. Most versions of Cobol require the payment of licence fees for the
use of executables on a commercial pc (ie one that is not the pc on
which the Cobol code was developed). You could use a free Cobol
compiler (tinycobol, Opencobol or one of the others that is out there)
but you would have to install it and the executable applications you
develop.

It makes more sense for a commercial enterprise to use Java or VB or C/
C++/C# (for all sorts of reasons including availability of developers
capable of supporting the package)

>
> Do enterprise companies use/prefer COBOL apps that run on a PC
> or a PC server, or the mainframes are here to dominate
> the enterprise market ?

Only if their applications already include Cobol based products.
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL to PC , makes sense ?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-03T04:26:33+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8lps5aFsmtU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<58e42b89-8a5b-4a2a-aaaf-e3a7c541575a@39g2000yqa.googlegroups.com>`

```
RealInfo wrote:
> Hi all
>
> Does it make sense to develop COBOL applications
> for the PC as final target ?

You've had some very sensible answers, from experienced people who know what 
they're talking about.

The question indicates that you don't really understand the PC environment.

Unlike a mainframe, a PC is almost pointless, unless it is connected to 
other PCs.

So applications are developed for the Network, not for a a single 
workstation or desktop. There WAS a time (a few years back), when it made 
sense to develop Windows applications for the desktop, (even for a single 
desktop) and do so in COBOL. Today that is more the realm of hobby 
programming than for serious commercial enterprise.

(Despite brave attempts by certain Companies with a vested interest to talk 
it up, the world isn't buying it and has voted with its feet. COBOL has less 
than 1%  (actually .4% 
http://www.tiobe.com/index.php/content/paperinfo/tpci/index.html  ) of the 
commercial development going on in the world today...it is now the 30th 
language for commercial development and has been dropped from reporting by a 
number of web sites which analyse language use and trends. Java has a 
seemingly unassailable lead as #1 with around 18% of the world's code 
currently being written in it. (C# around 6%))

COBOL does not really lend itself to Network programming (at least, not as 
well as some other languages), and this, combined with the  higher initial 
compiler costs, and run time fees if you distribute it, along with the 
higher cost of more labour intensive maintenance, has led to its decline as 
the Network programming language of choice.

Network programming is about objects and layers; programs are interrupt 
driven by events which occur and must be immediately responded to. This is a 
different paradigm from the Procedural COBOL language where the program 
decides when it will do a read or a write etc.

You CAN write COBOL programs for a PC and they will run just as if it is a 
"mini mainframe", but the question is increasingly becoming one of whether 
you would want to or not.

>
> Do enterprise companies use/prefer COBOL apps that run on a PC
> or a PC server, or the mainframes are here to dominate
> the enterprise market ?

Mainframes will increasingly become just another Network server (maybe with 
a load levelling priority to handle batch and Data Warehousing, UNLESS the 
TCO on them comes down. (It has considerably in the last 10 years, but there 
is still a gap between the most powerful Network servers and a top end 
commercial mainframe, in price if not in performance.) Of course, if the TCO 
achieves parity then there will be no real difference between a mainframe 
hooked into the Network and a Network server hooked into the Network. The 
fact that the architectures may be completely different is not a problem as 
far as the Network is concerned.
>
> I ask it because I do not own a mainframe I have to develop COBOL apps
> on a PC .

Hmmm... slavery has been abolished in most of the world for at least a 
century now.  WHAT or WHO is compelling you to write COBOL for a PC?

Could it be that you perceive computer programming as only being about COBOL 
so that MUST be the way to do it?

If that is the case and you want to do some "home programming" you can 
certainly do it in COBOL. But don't kid yourself it will be commercially 
viable unless you have a specific niche market and they really need your 
specialised  industry knowledge and skill. (As opposed ot programming 
knowledge and skill...)

I make a living at the moment largely based around helping people move OFF 
COBOL for their Network solutions. They don't make this brave Migration, 
with the consequent significant upheaval, so I can make a living. They do it 
because they are increasingly finding that maintaining COBOL for a Network 
environment is non-viable. Unanimously (in our exprience), people have found 
that their costs go down and their productivity goes up when they get off 
COBOL. (Once they have navigated the learning curve into a newer 
language/toolset.) My Company helps them do this without having to throw 
everything away and start over, and the tools we provide automatically 
generate a layered, object oriented solution from their existing  (Standard 
COBOL) applications.

If COBOL is what you know and love and you want to do some programming in 
it, and all you have available is a PC, you would do well to look at at 
products like Open COBOL and TinyCOBOL (as Alistair already advised you). 
They are free, but they have limitations.

For a Networked solution you will need an Object Oriented compiler and these 
are NOT free. (There are student versions available but again, these are 
limited.)

If you want to start a Home business writing commecrial software for PCs (or 
mobile devices like phones and PDAs - a vast emerging market...) look at the 
free downloads of C# and Visual Studio Express. There are free instructional 
videos available that will teach you the basics of both these products and 
the rest is up to you...

>
> Thanks in advance
> EC

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL to PC , makes sense ?

```
On Wed, 1 Dec 2010 10:31:48 -0800 (PST), RealInfo
<therightinfo@gmail.com> wrote:

>Hi all
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>EC

It only makes sense to develop COBOL apps for the PC if the company's
primary processing is done on a PC.  Otherwise, it doesn't make sense.
Apps can be developed on a PC to run on a mainframe up to a certain
point.  For example, it the primary purpose of the app is to randomly
read an online CICS VSAM master and return a screen containing
information from the record retrieved, that might not be an app you
would or could develop on a PC.

You could develop part of it, possibly, but to really test it and use
it, you would need a mainframe.  There is a CICS emulator for the PC,
so you could at least write those commands and test the syntax.

However, if the primary purpose of the app is to calculate interest
given a rate and a principle balance and a time frame, you could
develop that on the PC with a test file containing data to test the
calculation and then port all but the file access to the mainframe.


And ask yourself this, if you were going to re-wire your house, would
it make sense to read a book about plumbing because it talks about
doing the work yourself?  Or does it make more sense to read a book
about electricity in a house and its wiring?

Regards,
```

```
On Dec 13, 12:13 pm, duke <sidney.reilley...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 2, 9:31 am, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> TinyCobol's existence? Just curious?

From the OpenCOBOL site:

"""OpenCOBOL translates COBOL into C and compiles the translated code
using the native C compiler. You can build your COBOL programs on
various platforms, including Unix/Linux, Mac OS X, and Microsoft
Windows."""

The _purpose_ of OpenCOBOL is to translate COBOL into C and compile it
on various specified platforms, no more nor no less.

If you find it useful for what you wish to do then that is fine. If
you would like to help it achieve its purpose then I am sure they
would appreciate that.
```

```
On Dec 14, 1:33 am, duke <sidney.reilley...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 12, 7:41 pm, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[42 more quoted lines elided]…
> that it appears that most COBOL apps are running on mainframes, what

You start by making an unsupported claim. It may well be that more
lines of code are on mainframes, but you haven't defined how you would
measure 'most apps'. A bespoke mainframe application may run on just
one machine while a 'PC' apps may run on dozens or thousands of
machine. There may be many times more PCs running COBOL apps than
mainframes.

> would be the motivation to write OpenCOBOL which targets desktops, et
> al? I "get it" that some of the OpenCOBOL code could be massaged to
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> develop _for a PC_. No more nor no less. :D

COBOL has been available on 'Desktop PCs' since 1978 or so. On CP/M,
MP/M, OASYS, networked systems and others. Later on MS-DOS networks.
Companies such as MicroFocus, RM, Accu, sold the compilers and systems
to support these, as well as on Unix and VMS departmental minis.

The _purpose_ of OpenCOBOL is to compile COBOL to C. It happens that
it implements several features that are used in desktop applications,
and have been in commercial compilers for 30 years or more, such as X/
Open ACCEPT and DISPLAY.

It seems that many users of OpenCOBOL have developed for PCs for
decades and OpenCOBOL is another option. COBOL Web applications have
also been done for many years, I wrote my first in the mid 90s.
TinyCOBOL and OPenCOBOL have been used for that too.
```

Richard,
  I think you are being a bit pedantic and a bit misleading.  I don't know 
if software CAN have a 'purpose".  However, I think that it is fairly clear 
that the purpose of those developing OpenCOBOL is to provide a COBOL 
compiler for "the workstation". In no way and in no place do they seem to 
"promote" the use of the C code that is translated from the COBOL - as 
anything but an "intermediate" step toward wokstation applicaitons.

```
On Dec 14, 8:47 am, "Bill Klein" <wmkl...@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
wrote:
> Richard,
>   I think you are being a bit pedantic and a bit misleading.

In what way am I being 'misleading', or indeed 'pedantic'.

> I don't know if software CAN have a 'purpose".

Nor do I, but the project can have a purpose.

> However, I think that it is fairly clear
> that the purpose of those developing OpenCOBOL is to provide a COBOL
> compiler for "the workstation".

I provided a quote from the site that seemed to state _their_ purpose:

"""OpenCOBOL translates COBOL into C and compiles the translated code
using the native C compiler. You can build your COBOL programs on
various platforms, including Unix/Linux, Mac OS X, and Microsoft
Windows."""

> In no way and in no place do they seem to
> "promote" the use of the C code that is translated from the COBOL - as
> anything but an "intermediate" step toward wokstation applicaitons.

The importance of C is not in the result of the conversion from COBOL
source, but that this makes the application portable to a large number
of environments and also that it allows COBOL programs direct access
to a large number of libraries, eg GTK+. It also allows mixed language
programming such as with Vala.

For example OpenCOBOL can run on ARM processors on handhelds,
potentially on phones.

http://www.opencobol.org/modules/newbb/viewtopic.php?topic_id=1051&forum=1

On Unix/Linux systems the COBOL application can be run on the server
with ssh or putty access from anywhere or as web applications and AJAX
with browser access from almost anything.

It is you that is being 'pedantic and misleading' by claiming that
OpenCOBOL is limited to 'workstation applications' when the developers
make no such limitations.



> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[79 more quoted lines elided]…
> TinyCOBOL and OPenCOBOL have been used for that too.

```
Bill Klein wrote:
> Richard,
>  I think you are being a bit pedantic and a bit misleading.  I don't
> know if software CAN have a 'purpose".

Gee, Bill, if it doesn't, have I wasted my life...? :-)

I don't think there is any doubt that  software, being simply computer code, 
cannot have a purpose. Purpose requires intention and that requires 
sentience... However, there is also no doubt that people have a purpose in 
writing it and using it(otherwise no-one would be writing it for money...). 
I think that the perceived purpose may vary depending on who is looking at 
it. The writer may see the purpose as: "gain experience, try out some new 
ideas, make a few quid..." or even "let's see if we can make life easier for 
these people", etc...; the purchaser may see the purpose as: "Sort out my 
accounts, get better control of my inventory, get a competitive advantage, 
etc". In this scenario the purpose that Richard described seems valid to me, 
even if it is not the ONLY purpose for having OpenCOBOL.


However, I think that it is
> fairly clear that the purpose of those developing OpenCOBOL is to
> provide a COBOL compiler for "the workstation". In no way and in no
> place do they seem to "promote" the use of the C code that is
> translated from the COBOL - as anything but an "intermediate" step
> toward wokstation applicaitons.

I'd agree that development of workstation applications was probably behind 
it, but if that were the sole reason, then why generate C code? Why not 
generate Assembler in the conventional way? I think the people who did it 
were interested in using C for a reason. It may have been because they felt 
familiar with C and wanted to show it was up to the task, it might have been 
because they wanted the benefits of using C run time without the complexity 
of writing C, or it might have been for totally unrelated reasons which we 
cannot fathom.

Whatever the reason, the fact that OpenCOBOL uses C does lend some credence 
to Richard's POV.

(Interesting to think of OO COBOL as a "poor man's C compiler" ... :-))

<snipped unreferenced previous>

Pete.
```

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** "robertwessel2@yahoo.com" <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-14T02:45:28-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8e962ed8-81d6-489a-ae46-16355c606e36@n10g2000yqd.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<58e42b89-8a5b-4a2a-aaaf-e3a7c541575a@39g2000yqa.googlegroups.com> <03iff6phofdhdm0alttu4dhopmkg0m3lud@4ax.com> <659625e6-d211-41c2-9c64-fb042b56e937@g26g2000vba.googlegroups.com> <967844e7-4ae5-4fc2-929c-4389cc2ddfd3@j19g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <3b801d6b-538d-42d9-bc55-e29d233bc096@r40g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <aec9cc9c-4019-4de1-b17a-910ea66d9eb8@37g2000prx.googlegroups.com> <jWuNo.40207$UC6.5393@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <8mo6luFfbbU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Dec 13, 9:30 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Bill Klein wrote:
> > fairly clear that the purpose of those developing OpenCOBOL is to
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> to Richard's POV.


Errr...  Using C as an intermediate target is *hugely* popular, and
for very good reasons.  Many high level structures can be mapped well
to C, C compilers are ubiquitous, and with a bit of effort the
generated C code can be highly portable, eliminating the need to write
complex back ends for every target you'd like to compile code for.
All things which do not happen if you generate assembler or machine
code directly.

Writing back ends is *hard*.  Writing *good* back ends (IOW, ones that
generate good code) is *very* hard.  Even reusing preexisting back
ends in a (reasonably) well defined structure (like in GCC) is pretty
hard (and if your language requires things that the existing back ends
don’t support well, you stuck either patching around it, or trying to
convince a bunch of people to add support for something in a multitude
of back ends) – which is not to say it doesn’t happen, look at all the
front ends in GCC (although the OpenCOBOL folks have clearly decided
not to tie themselves that closely to GCC)..

In the distant past Fortran was a popular target, although mainly
because of its (then) ubiquity (C is a better target in almost all
respects, much of what is valuable about Fortran has to be worked
around in that role).

These days you also see a bit of C++, too, and a smattering of other
languages, but C is by far the most common.

And *nobody* intends for that intermediate C code to ever really be
visible outside the innards of the compiler (although you often see
remnants in the debugger).  The intermediate C code tends to be
really, really ugly, and almost unintelligible.  In fact it often
stresses C compilers in severe and unusual ways (is your C compiler
choking on  a switch statement with 5000 cases, or a function with
30,000 lines?  You can bet that code came from a code generator).  But
this intermediate C code is not like the code generated by
translators, which are intended to allow the programmers to switch to
the new language (although most of those don't generate particularly
good or readable code either).

FWIW, the first C++ was a front end translator to C.

The clear purpose of OpenCOBOL is to compile Cobol programs.  That a C
compiler is needed as part of the infrastructure is immaterial.
```

```
Sorry for the LATE reply to this note.

I agree that the original purpose of the OpenCOBOL (and probably other) 
projects was to provide an "Open Source COBOL compiler - and run-time".  At 
the time that most of these project began, there was an (unwritten) rule 
that

  Open Source = Written in C and targetted at Unix (or possibly Linux)

When I use the term "workstation," I tend to think that this includes (but 
is not limited to) Linux/Unix environments (with or without "Servers").

So restating by misleading assertions, the intent of the "Open Source COBOL" 
projects (including OpenCOBOL) was to produce a free (and user-maintainable 
and customizable) COBOL compiler and run-time environment that would allo 
COBOL applications to run in "open operating systems and their 
environments".

Written in C and producing C "intermediate" source code was a SIDE-EFFECT of 
the desire to create a vfree (and open source) COBOL compiler.

The converse was NOT true (as I understand such things) that the intent was 
to produce a COBOL compiler in written in C and which produced C 
intermediate code - for the "sake" of having C source and code. (compiler or 
application code).

To a "mainframe biggot" (which historically I am - as were/are many other 
COBOL users) intending to provide an "open source" COBOL compiler was 
equivalent to (if not identcal with) producing a COBOL compiler and run-time 
for "workstation" environments.  As time as gone along, this equivalence has 
both diverged and differed - depending on users' actual needs and desires.


"Bernard Giroud" <bgiroud3@nospam.free.fr> wrote in message

On Dec 28, 10:03 pm, "Bill Klein" <wmkl...@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
wrote:
> Sorry for the LATE reply to this note.
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>   Open Source = Written in C and targetted at Unix (or possibly Linux)

That is a very narrow view. There has been large amounts of open
source aimed at CP/M, MS-DOS and even Windows. This was mostly
assembler, BASIC and Pascal. There are whole open source operating
systems, such as BeOS based Haiku. Much open source over the last
decade or two is Perl, Python or PHP.

> When I use the term "workstation," I tend to think that this includes (but
> is not limited to) Linux/Unix environments (with or without "Servers").

The term is commonly used as it is given in Wikipedia: "A workstation
is a high-end microcomputer designed for technical or scientific
applications. Intended primarily to be used by one person at a
time, ..."

It may be that 35 years ago (mid 70s) 'workstation' was almost
synonymous with 'single user computer' but since then there have been
a variety of other machines: microcomputers, personal computers
(starting with Apple II), consoles, handhelds, laptops, netbooks,
smartphones, ...

> So restating by misleading assertions, the intent of the "Open Source COBOL"
> projects (including OpenCOBOL) was to produce a free (and user-maintainable
> and customizable) COBOL compiler and run-time environment that would allo
> COBOL applications to run in "open operating systems and their
> environments".

I am not sure how you have gathered this 'intent', perhaps you could
point out where it is stated by the OpenCOBOL project.

As it happens it also runs on Mac and Windows, and possibly others.

> Written in C and producing C "intermediate" source code was a SIDE-EFFECT of
> the desire to create a vfree (and open source) COBOL compiler.

It certainly is not inconsequential. It is fundamental to producing a
portable compiler and run-time and also provides for utilizing many
libraries such as curses and various isam file systems that are
already available on a large number of systems.

> The converse was NOT true (as I understand such things) that the intent was
> to produce a COBOL compiler in written in C and which produced C
> intermediate code - for the "sake" of having C source and code. (compiler or
> application code).

How have you determined this ? The C code is certainly 'intermediate
code' as thus not an end in itself, the "sake" of having C code is
stated on the site as: "OpenCOBOL translates COBOL into C and compiles
the translated code using the native C compiler. You can build your
COBOL programs on various platforms, including Unix/Linux, Mac OS X,
and Microsoft Windows."


> To a "mainframe biggot" (which historically I am - as were/are many other
> COBOL users) intending to provide an "open source" COBOL compiler was
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It may well be that in the 70s there were 'mainframes', 'departmental
minis' and new-fangled  'workstations', but that was not when the
OpenCOBOL project formed.
```

On 12/6/2010 11:34 AM, Alistair Maclean wrote:
> MiroFocus are showcasing their release 3 version of Visual Cobol:
>
> http://blog.microfocus.com/visual-cobol-r3-technology-preview/569/

Interesting video if you like 'marketing' stuff. I had a mild laugh. 
They showed a map of N. America with red pins (Google style), indicating 
sites in US. Then back-tracked to 2008, fewer pins in US and one showed 
up in the Great White North. Surely can't be a reference to li'l old me 
? Nah. Most likely the software house in Calgary using Net Express and 
now owned by Fujitsu :-).

I wont be issuing second part of the Rosetta 99-Bottles - figure it out 
for yourself from below. Actually the complete programming was completed 
when I issued my Bottle-Count. The hold back, getting the hang of 
diagramming in MS Word and preparing write-ups. As is, the whole thing 
works, (excluding the accompanying Readme.docs, still in draft).

I think they are a pretty interesting and active COBOL bunch in Brazil, 
and reading about President Lula finishing his term, the article I saw 
made reference that Brazil was one of the four up-and-coming economies, 
China, India, ? and Brazil - the latter, they have offshore oil, (as do 
the Chinese). From the M/F Forum I knew that Altair in Brazil was 
preparing a book on Webbing - looks like he's got it done. Just wrote 
this to him today having gotten back into the M/F Forum, (with some 
difficulty) :-

*-----------------------------------------------------------------------
Date:	 Wednesday, December 08, 2010 03:37 PM

Congratulations on getting the book out
Altair. Wouldn't do me much good until I can
get it in English, although I was able to
translate your web page into English.

However...........

1. If you check out from the M/F Home Page
everything prior to N/E V.5.1 is no longer
supported by M/F. In fact N/E V 5.1. has
almost reached the end of its shelf life. The
new baby is M/F's Visual COBOL 2010, which
I'll come to later. And that's why 5.1. is NO
LONGER down loadable. I tried, got the setup
Executable, but on entering 'Run' doesn't do
anything, nor give an error message.

2. As part of the above, M/F reserve the
right, 'at their discretion' to delete
messages no longer relevant. I'm looking at
TWO messages only as I reply to you. All
previous entries appear to have been deleted.

(*** Note they must have a program screw-up, it showed some 6,000 
previous entries when I posted this, but again only three entries when I 
went back to my message to include it here in CLC ***)

3. M/F are putting their money on Visual
COBOL 2010. You can currently download, in
this order :-

- (1) M/S Visual Studio and then,
- (2) M/F Visual COBOL 2010 (R2 = Release 2)

4. You can see a 'marketing' video on their
Release # 3 - see :-

http://blog.microfocus.com/visual-cobol-r3-
technology-preview/569/

5. I have done # 3 above and it downloaded
easily. Then you have to get into the concept
of M/S .Net. Of course from familiarity, you
get there in time, but being brand new to
most of us, it isn't as intuitive as the N/E
IDE.

6. Even if you don't use it - download it and
check out their Legal Statement - boy have
the legal beagles made an attempt to tie us
up in knots !

7. For your Web book are you using any M/F
classes for Webbing - if so forget it. I had
to check back, but I sent you an early
version of using MyDialog.cbl with N/E V 3.1.
Quite simply - my demo wont work in Visual
COBOL.

8. Haven't found my way through Visual COBOL
- but I do know the following :-

EXCLUSIONS - the new product does NOT contain
anything about (a) Dialog Editor and GUI
Classes, or (b) Dialog System. Further a
quick check on N/E V 3.1. and Visual COBOL R2
shows the following number of OO Classes :-

COBOL source (approx): Net Express : 245	
Visual COBOL 2010 : 54

Net Express V 3.1 : I restricted the search
to the sub-folders for Class source, i.e.
excluding things like demo programs.

Only briefly familiar with the new product, I
did a search in \Program Files\Visual Studio
2010 for *ALL* files with the extension
"*.CBL".

While there appear to be no OO references to
GUI-ing they did retain the sub-group 'Lists'
(Arrays/CharacterArray/Collections/Dictionari
es) in the 54 above. Can't understand why
they would do that and exclude GUIs.

9. So you want to do GUI-ing - you have got
to make a decision. You make a choice, (or
both if you want to fiddle), between Visual
Basic and C#. And that is not clear cut -
google on 'Visual Basic versus C#'.

We are talking about a very different
product; bye, bye to backwards compatibility.
And at this point in development - price
hasn't been mentioned !

*>---------------------------------------------------

While the new product does wonders, in view of my comments above, do I 
need to say more, or why regrettably I've decided to call it quits with 
COBOL.

Jimmy, Calgary AB

It doesn't need them, Jimmy. The .NET Framework provides a superior GUI 
experience. You can see it in action if you looked at my demo of the COBOL 
Projects Management tool: 
http://primacomputing.co.nz/PRIMAWebSite/BatchCompile.aspx

The .NET controls you use to build your GUI have slicker rendering, more 
properties you can access and are generally faster than DIALOG.

> Further a
> quick check on N/E V 3.1. and Visual COBOL R2
> shows the following number of OO Classes :-

>
> COBOL source (approx): Net Express : 245
> Visual COBOL 2010 : 54

Largely sample code?

Again, The .NET environment is widely documented. Once you get the hang of 
using objects it is easy to "translate" to COBOL, although most examples are 
in C#, VB.NET, and C++.
>

…[12 more quoted lines elided]…

Lists and collections are not part of the GUI  (GUI controls may use node 
collections etc. but they are also used elsewhere.)

>
> 9. So you want to do GUI-ing - you have got
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> google on 'Visual Basic versus C#'.

That's interesting. Are you saying they haven't provided .NET Form support 
for Visual COBOL?

It seems unlikely. I suspect you are a bit bewildered by VS at this stage... 
:-) If you go to FILE > NEW > PROJECT  it should bring upa dialogue box that 
has a tree view in the left pane with an entry for COBOL. Under that it 
should say (amongst other offerings) "Windows". Select that and the right 
pane will show the installed templates you can use. Click on "Windows Forms 
Application" and click OK. This will open a blank form in design view. You 
make sure your Toolbox is visible (Check the "View" menu) and then you can 
drag and drop controls onto it. A lot depends on how you have configured VS 
and that is why I strongly recommend that people do the basic videos which 
explain what the various views are for, how to dock panes and hide them, and 
what the effect of right and left clicking does. To provide event code for 
your controls just double click the control and the view switches to code 
view where you can cobble away in COBOL to your heart's content. :-)

>
> We are talking about a very different
> product;

Yep, you got that right! (It is actually a much better product, but it takes 
some time for that to become apparent...)



> bye, bye to backwards compatibility.
> And at this point in development - price
> hasn't been mentioned !

You can't take a cuddle blanket into the Brave New World, Jimmy. There is a 
whole generation of PowerCOBOL applications that are incompatible too, it 
isn't just DIALOG. Oddly enough, a couple of years back I asked if anyone 
was interested in a tool to convert PowerCOBOL (and, later, DIALOG) 
applications to .NET and the result was a deafening silence. As it would 
have involved a considerable investment on my part I decided not to do it. I 
think that most people are either writing off their old COBOL development 
entirely, or, where they can't/won't do that they are refactoring what they 
can and moving to a different language.

It is a vindication of tiered development (Objects and Layers); systems that 
have a Presentation Layer separate from the Business Layer have little 
problem with how the GUI is implemented, or WHAT it is implemented in. The 
lessons learned in mainframe development 40 years ago are still as true as 
they ever were. CICS and IMS/DC applications can be moved to .NET 
comparatively easily because both systems use a data buffer between the 
screen and the application. You don't actually NEED a CICS emulator to 
migrate mainframe applications, but it does save a lot of work that would 
otherwise be required in manually defining existing screen layouts for Win 
Forms or WPF.

(I'm not advocating that mainframe COBOL applications SHOULD be migrated 
directly to .NET; just saying that it is easier to do than some PC legacy 
COBOL applications. Ideally, ANYTHING going to .NET should be "Objectified" 
first and separation layers for at least 3 tiers (Presentation, Business 
logic, and Database) should be implemented. The PRIMA Migration Toolset 
generates the separation layer between Business Logic and Database 
automatically, however, as noted above, I decided not to implement an 
automated separation for the Presentation Layer because no-one appeared to 
be interested, and it is a huge amount of work. It took me a couple of years 
to write the Database separation and generate Objects that can be used for 
data access, but for that I had clients who were very keen to move off ISAM 
to RDB and it was therefore worth the effort.)
>
> *>---------------------------------------------------
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> with COBOL.

I gather you are disappointed at the fact that the new world doesn't 
encompass the old one in the way you might have hoped?

Understandable. I had similar feelings when I first decided to get away from 
COBOL.

(Sadly, for many people, they can't/won't bite the bullet and they don't 
seem to be aware that the longer they leave it, the more it costs. Moving 
off COBOL is no longer a question of IF it is a question of WHEN. Eventually 
it will be impossible to get COBOL expertise at an economic price. If the 
applications are such that they can be replaced by packages or outsourced 
data services then there may be an option (still more expensive than moving 
off COBOL sooner) but if those options are simply not there and the company 
is "locked in" to their Legacy applications... (I wouldn't like to be the 
CTO who had to tell the Board he allowed that to happen.))

I only moved because I really had no option; the treatment I received from 
the COBOL vendor was simply unacceptable. (To me, at least...YMMV)

But, there is always a silver lining. I realise now, they did me a favour, 
and I bear no malice. (If they offered me the Enterprise Version of their 
latest .NET COBOL for free, I would say "No, thanks."  (I was made this 
offer by a party other than the vendor, and declined.) COBOL simply isn't 
relevant in the world I now inhabit.

I don't actually believe you are giving up COBOL, Jimmy, but perhaps you 
could take some time out and just have a look at VS and C#...?

I'm not sure if the COBOL implementation of Visual COBOL requires VS to be 
dedicated. (Most versions support C#, VB.NET, and C++, but COBOL may be 
different.  If it does, you would need to do a download of Visual C#)

I just spent some time searchng the web to try and find the videos I used 
initially but I think they may have been withdrawn. Nevertheless, there are 
over a million sites offering free videos... Have a look at: 
http://idealprogrammer.com/

If you progress with this let us know how you get on.

I can't see you sitting back twiddling your thumbs :-)

Pete.

Alistair Maclean wrote:
> MiroFocus are showcasing their release 3 version of Visual Cobol:
>
> http://blog.microfocus.com/visual-cobol-r3-technology-preview/569/

I just had a look at it.

You really should...

It is quite unbelievable.

Senior Managers saying things like: "I can demonstrate to a customer without 
having to write a single line of code..."

Er... ever noticed that any application you run does that?

(This was based on a C# demonstration from MS where they showed a data 
source being dropped on  an ASP.NET page. It is trivial and at least 7 years 
old. Now you can do it in MF  Visual COBOL (as long as you use VS which is 
where the "magic" occurs...) and at least one guy at MF is very excited 
about it...)

Best of the lot was the Android phone demo, showing COBOL handling events 
for a GUI.

"The cat goes 'Miaow'", "The bat goes 'Squeak'", "Look! When I touch the 
button it shows the noise the animal makes..." (notice the device has the 
capability to make the actual sound, but instead we see it as text on the 
screen...) "Wow! And you have an event driven GUI responding in COBOL on a 
mobile phone... that has to be the future of COBOL..."... "This is just like 
a mainframe running on a mobile phone..."   (I'm not making this up, watch 
it yourself...)

As some of us were doing this 15 years ago in PowerCOBOL (admittedly not on 
an Android phone, but that is just a desktop simulator.) forgive my yawn... 
It works because they ported the JVM stack so the COBOL can be wrapped in 
Java...which Android is written in and which is currently subject to a multi 
billion dollar lawsuit over the legality of the Android linux distribution 
using parts of the Apache Harmony component which ORACLE is claiming is NOT 
GPL licensed and violates their IP.... The same code WON'T run on Win Phone 
7 because that is not currently programmed in Java. (Guess what it IS 
programmed in? No, not COBOL...)

At least if you develop for Win Phone 7 there is no risk of the code being 
illegal...

Overall, this is one of the most embarrassing promotions of COBOL I have 
ever seen. I actually squirmed for them.

And, although they claimed they wrote loads of sample code, they didn't show 
us any of it... Wonder why?

1000 man hours -  across continents.   Give me those resources; I reckon I 
can do better than "The Bat goes squeak..." It is like watching people get 
excited over turning the washing machine on and finding that it works, or 
using a telephone for the first time. You're happy for them but kind of 
embarrassed at the same time. If it was your kids you'd be proud, but these 
are computer professionals, in 2010...

I'm sorry this had the effect on me that it did. The problem is not with the 
product (it is excellent), rather it is with people being really excited 
over something that is taken as a matter of course if you DON'T use COBOL.

The only bit that made sense to me was the injunction to "Let's all go down 
the pub!"

I think I'd be spending 3 days there after this exercise.

Pete.

On Dec 10, 3:40 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Alistair Maclean wrote:
> > MiroFocus are showcasing their release 3 version of Visual Cobol:
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> GPL licensed and violates their IP....

You misrepresent the situation. Android uses a Dalvik VM which is not
related at all to the Sun/Oracle Java VM. It happens that the Dalvik
language is more or less Java. Whether Apache uses the GPL is
irrelevant, the Apache licence for Harmony allows Google to use it.

The case is primarily about patents. Oracle claims that it has patents
on Java VM. Google claims that the Dalvik VM doesn't work the same way
as JVM and the patents do not apply. There is one file that Oracle
claims copyright infringement on.

Anyway Android has several other languages available, including Python
and Lua.

(My pocket camera runs Lua scripts, see CHDK)

> The same code WON'T run on Win Phone
> 7 because that is not currently programmed in Java. (Guess what it IS
> programmed in? No, not COBOL...)

Variations of Silverlight and XNA.

"""Increase your application revenues with the new Microsoft
Advertising SDK for Windows Phone 7. The SDK allow easy integration of
text and banner ads into your application, and consumes ads served by
Microsoft's mobile Ad Exchange, the first bidded ad exchange for
mobile. The exchange includes multiple advertiser sales channels
competing in real-time to purchase your ad inventory, incuding
Microsoft’s national sales force and adCenter’s significant market
footprint. Microsoft's best-in-class ad targeting solution means
better click through rates and better monetization, and the new SDK is

of how ads are performing in your applications."""

Hi Pete,

Thanks for taking the time to review the video. We were having some
fun (just because we use COBOL doesn't mean we have to be boring) and
clearly the demos being shown don't really help solve world hunger
(although that's on the roadmap ;-) ). We will be making the sample
source code available around the time of the Visual COBOL R3 launch in
the New Year along with other new support for the COBOL developer
community.

There are lots of things we're working based on some of the technology
shown in the video - it's where the dots start getting joined up that
things get interesting. For example, we can take a single COBOL source
file and deploy it as managed code on .NET and JVM and Azure and
Amazon and Android and WP7 and ... without translating source code,
wrapping it with other languages or other unnatural acts. This isn't
something that many language systems can do, certainly not with the
enterprise performance and reliability of COBOL and not while also
allowing existing production code that's delivering business value
every day to be reused without change.

Anyway, I've already drunk the Koolaid so the above doesn't
necessarily count for very much :-) I'll let others have their own
view of whether this is good stuff or not.

Mark Warren
Micro Focus

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Dec 10, 9:56 am, MarkW <markwarr...@gmail.com> wrote:
>> On Dec 9, 2:40 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
…[78 more quoted lines elided]…
> What about DB2 and IMS access? And CICS?

DB2 is easily available through ODBC. I'm not sure about IMS... need to 
search... hang on...

iways will let you access IMS using SQL (their middleware translates it to 
DL/1 calls) : http://www.iwaysoftware.com/products/adapters/ims.html

MF Mainframe Express and CA VISION both provide full access to IMS, but it 
isn't clear whether Mainframe Express can run with Visual COBOL.(I'd be 
surprised if it couldn't, but  would happily undertake to make it do so if 
someone pays me enough...I have very extensive experience with InterOP 
Services for .Net... :-))

I couldn't find a simple IMS interface that would work like an ODBC driver, 
but that doesn't mean there aren't any (I only searched for a few minutes 
and there were thousands of results.)

CICS is pretty much irrelevant in the .Net environment because .NET provides 
a far superior GUI and interrupt driven processing. However, there would be 
a fair bit of work in converting CICS BMS to .NET Forms or WPF. This would 
probably be addressed by using a CICS Emulator and there are quite a few of 
these from various vendors.

Pete.

```

>
> > What about DB2 and IMS access? And CICS?
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

MW> Mainframe DB2, CICS and IMS compatibility is delivered with the
Studio Enterprise Edition products from Micro Focus. DB2 off-mainframe
is supported in Visual COBOL via ODBC but if you want mainframe
compatibility then Studio EE has the "Host Compatibiity Option" which
provides mainframe compatibility for both UDB and SQL Server. Studio
EE/Server EE has great CICS and IMS compatibility. Both are supported
in native code with Visual Studio 2008 and managed CICS is provided in
VS2010 - IMS, JCL etc will be added over the next few releases.

By the way - Mainframe Express is intended for development and testing
of applications that will be deployed back on zOS. Studio EE is for
applications that are being migrated from z/OS to Windows, Linux and
UNIX. MFE does not have a Visual Studio integration at present but
over the next year of so, they will be combined and hosted in Visual
Studio.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: MicroFocus and Visual Cobol

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-11T18:49:57+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8mgho7Fk16U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<bbdd0e70-be5a-48c0-9846-60e3c8d3481f@m35g2000vbn.googlegroups.com> <8mc838Fg36U1@mid.individual.net> <346caeeb-25b1-4f3b-a279-34d3b5e8da03@s5g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>`

```
MarkW wrote:
> On Dec 9, 2:40 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[57 more quoted lines elided]…
> wrapping it with other languages or other unnatural acts.

C# compiled for Mono or .Net can use XMLVM to run on the JVM. It is a 
standard tool available today without further development.



> This isn't
> something that many language systems can do,

Only the ones that generate CLR... :-) (that would be ALL of the FREE .Net 
languages... )

> certainly not with the
> enterprise performance and reliability of COBOL

ROFL!

In a very lengthy and varied career, I have seen many cases where COBOL code 
for the enterprise was neither reliable nor performing well...

It is programmers and programming which assure "enterprise performance and 
reliability" NOT the languages they use...



> and not while also
> allowing existing production code that's delivering business value
> every day to be reused without change.

So you advocate compiling COBOL to CLR without change?

I consider that a recipe for disaster, but I don't work for anybody 
promoting such a compiler. :-)

>
> Anyway, I've already drunk the Koolaid so the above doesn't
> necessarily count for very much :-) I'll let others have their own
> view of whether this is good stuff or not.

You have every bit as much right to post your views here as I have, and I 
enjoyed your post.
>
> Mark Warren
> Micro Focus

Thanks for a good response and not taking my reaction personally, Mark. As 
always, I call 'em like I see 'em; sometimes that isn't  what people want to 
hear and over the years I have been in trouble here on numerous occasions.

These days, it tends to be less fierce that it was. Maybe we're all getting 
old (or better informed...) :-)

If I had to sum up COBOL running in .Net (whether the Fujitsu 
implementation, or your own company's) this is what I would want to know:

1. What can be done in Visual COBOL that can't be done in C# or Java? 
(Assuming use of Eclipse or VS 2010 as the IDE).

Is anybody game to show how many of the "New features" of Visual COBOL are 
actually provided by or dependent on support from  VS 2010 and how many are 
truly new COBOL features? (If I was actually anti MF I'd publish such a 
table, but I'm not, so I won't, and I don't really expect you to either :-) 
It is just a point, that's all...)

Given that the answer is: "Nothing.", then why would I use COBOL? (It is an 
expensive compiler and takes a lot more writing and maintaining.)

 If your answer to that is: "Because you can use a language you already know 
without having to learn a new one..." then I would have to reply: 
"Bollocks!"

OO COBOL is as foreign to most COBOL people as C++ or Assembler. You may 
have seen posts here from COBOL guys bitterly bewailing that it "no longer 
looks like COBOL." The video made a big deal about interrupt driven code in 
COBOL because MOST COBOL people have no idea how that works and think it 
must be "clever". That's because they don't understand Objects or OO COBOL. 
(If they did, they would recognise  Encapsulation, Objects, Properties, 
Methods, and Events...) And yet, to utilize .Net effectively, the code needs 
to be refactored as objects. (EVERYTHING in .Net is a Class, and Classes get 
instantiated as Objects...)

2. Given that C# and Java are FREE and Visual COBOL is "expensive", what 
added value will I get by using COBOL rather than C# or Java?

3. If you are suggesting I can just recompile my existing applications and 
they will run effectively in .Net, why have I found serious performance 
degradation of everything when I do this? Why am I having to buy new servers 
since I moved to .Net COBOL? Isn't it supposed to be better and faster?

4. Why wasn't I told from day 1 that unless I "objectify" my existing code 
it will never perform well on .Net?

OK, the last 2 are probably unfair, but you catch my drift?

I have been using OO COBOL for around 15 years and developing for .Net for 
about 4. I had over 20 years of COBOL before that, but still found OO COBOL 
an unintelligible mess to wade through. (It was only when I took time out to 
teach myself Java that it all fell into place.)

Here is the syllogism that sums up my argument against NetCOBOL for .Net or 
Visual COBOL (compilers that generate CLR):

1. The network (as represented by .Net, where EVERYTHING is a Class...) 
requires Objects and Layers to function effectively.
2. Using a CLR generating COBOL compiler to simply recompile legacy doesn't 
address the question of Objects and Layers.
3. I CAN use a standard OO COBOL compiler (which I already have) to wrap my 
COBOL as Objects.
4. I know I'm going to move off COBOL at some point, so why invest in an 
expensive CLR generating COBOL compiler when I don't need it and am moving 
away from COBOL anyway?

CONCLUSION:  CLR generating COBOL compilers are an expensive and unnecessary 
waste of time and resource. It would be better spent retraining my people 
(or picking up new ones) to use a .Net language like C# or VB.NET

I chose to go with C#. All my new stuff runs efficiently on .Net AND so does 
my legacy COBOL which HAS NOT been recompiled to CLR.

(To be fair, all of my development has been component based for the past 20 
years, so I may have had an easier time than if I had large monolithic 
integrated COBOL structures.)

Please don't draw the wrong conclusion from this response.

I think Visual COBOL is an excellent implementation (so is the competing 
NetCOBOL for .Net, for that matter...) But, so far, NONE of these offerings 
have recognised that NETWORK programming is NOT like traditional mainframe 
programming and advised their customers to do some refactoring BEFORE 
migrating. And NONE of these compilers are actually necessary to run COBOL 
code in .Net.

PRIMA has now made a number of migrations (including our own) from 
Procedural COBOL to .Net and in every case we have provided tools to create 
Objects and Layers as part of that Migration. In NO case did we find COBOL 
compiled to CLR to be required, or even useful.

The only exception I can see to this would be where a client was committed 
to staying wth COBOL as their major development language. In that case only 
would I recommend they purchase a COBOL CLR generating compiler. In all our 
cases, the clients have wanted to move OFF COBOL as soon as practicable.

We are about to undertake another "moderate" size migration and will provide 
a "Proof of Concept" in January. (I LOVE POCs! They are so easy to do, show 
that we can deliver what we promise, and usually leave the client with an 
astonished look... :-)) I have already advised the client against purchasing 
a COBOL CLR compiler...

see http://primacomputing.co.nz for details of our approach.

Pete.
```

MW> This falls into my "unnatural acts" category - how many emulators/

debuggers and other tools be if they're not directly connecting the
source code to execution platform? Certainly something similar can be
achieved with enough bits band-aided together, but is that the right
way to work?

>

…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> reliability" NOT the languages they use...

MW> True, but some languages make it harder (or easier). We've got

into Java because they couldn't get the performance or accuracy they
needed. That's not to say Java or CSharp are always the wrong answer,
more that COBOL gets an unnecessary bad rap too many times.

>

…[7 more quoted lines elided]…

>

> (Assuming use of Eclipse or VS 2010 as the IDE).

MW> Reuse of existing code shouldn't be underestimated. There are
tasks, especially financial calculations that are easier in COBOL than
Java and CSharp especially if trying to get the same accuracy as
COBOL.

>
> Is anybody game to show how many of the "New features" of Visual COBOL are
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> expensive compiler and takes a lot more writing and maintaining.)

MW> It's not nothing but it's perfectly fine that a lot of the
features that are now available to COBOL programmers are provided by
Visual Studio (or Eclipse) - these were features they couldn't really
use very well or not at all (if they were using Net Express or Server
Express or even other vendors implementations of COBOL in VS or
Eclipse). There are features that are specific to COBOL and we'll be
adding to them over time but just making sure the best tools are
available for COBOL is still good news. (Tis is especially true with
the language changes we've been making).

>
>  If your answer to that is: "Because you can use a language you already know
> without having to learn a new one..." then I would have to reply:
> "Bollocks!"
>
MW> Anglo-saxon like a native :-)

> OO COBOL is as foreign to most COBOL people as C++ or Assembler. You may
> have seen posts here from COBOL guys bitterly bewailing that it "no longer
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> instantiated as Objects...)

MW> There's some truth in this and it's something we're concerned
about. We're working on training classes and other new materials to
help educate existing COBOL programmers how to use OO. However there's
also a lot of mileage in pragmatically adding OO and .NET features to
traditional procedural COBOL. It may not win many academic prizes for
programming model purity but it's a very productive way forward. The
other benefit is that there's a shortage of trained COBOL programmers
with or without OO skills. Having a modern OO language that's not a
million miles from CSharp or Java makes it a lot easier for those
programmers to be productive with COBOL (that's what the users are
saying, not just us).

>
> 2. Given that C# and Java are FREE and Visual COBOL is "expensive", what
> added value will I get by using COBOL rather than C# or Java?

MW> Well, when you say "free" there are many different definitions of
that price. We've got to be able to match that and clearly deliver
value for paid languages, runtimes, frameworks etc. We're working on
it.

>
> 3. If you are suggesting I can just recompile my existing applications and
> they will run effectively in .Net, why have I found serious performance
> degradation of everything when I do this? Why am I having to buy new servers
> since I moved to .Net COBOL? Isn't it supposed to be better and faster?

MW> This is definitely a "your mileage may vary" story. The JVM
compiler has new optimizations that we haven't yet retrofitted to
the .NET compiler. In many situations we've got code that running
faster on JVM than as native code. Overall we're not seeing
significant slow downs with managed code in real use.

>
> 4. Why wasn't I told from day 1 that unless I "objectify" my existing code
> it will never perform well on .Net?

MW> Who said that? Our focus has been on procedural code running
on .NET and JVM. The new syntax is needed not just to modernize that
code but also for the work we do internally (most of our development
for the tools etc. is in COBOL).

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> teach myself Java that it all fell into place.)

MarkW wrote:
> Thanks for the comments Pete. The format of newsgroup messages with
> multiple levels of quotation is doing my head in so sorry if I get
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> source?

Why would you want to pay to have managed code from COBOL source? You can 
run your COBOL code as unmanaged and get managed code from a free compiler 
that was designed with objects in mind.

Microsoft realised at the time they designed .Net that there would be a 
large amount of legacy code that people would want to run on the Framework. 
They provided InterOP services for exactly the purpose of seamlessly 
integrating legacy with managed code, and I can state from personal 
experience that it is really quite remarkable. Native code under InterOP 
services is actually more efficient than managed code, and despite the fact 
that it isn't "managed", it can be wrapped with a managed wrapper so that 
exceptions are still trapped as they are for managed code.

The REAL question is simply: "Do I want to move off COBOL?"

If the answer is "yes", you don't need a CLR generating COBOL compiler and 
should probsably re-invest the money you save into training for the .Net 
platform and languages.

If the answer is "no", then a CLR generating compiler MAY be useful, but 
sooner or later you are going to have to transition, and the longer you 
leave it the more it will cost you. As proficiency and understanding of the 
.Net platform and languages grows, it will become increasingly apparent that 
writing COBOL for it is like wading through treacle, compared to using a 
.Net language. Eventually programmers simply vote with their feet.


> How well can the code be optimized or how good can the
> debuggers and other tools be if they're not directly connecting the
> source code to execution platform? Certainly something similar can be
> achieved with enough bits band-aided together, but is that the right
> way to work?

Suppose for a moment you had "legacy" (unmanaged code) in VB and COBOL, 
maybe a few Intel Assembler routines, perhaps even some legacy Fortran 
modules. ALL of them will run under InterOP services and you can step 
through and debug in the usual way until you hit the unmanaged code. 
Depending on WHAT that code is, you may or may not be able to debug it on 
the platform. I have run the Fujitsu COBOL debugger (which can be set up to 
activate when a certain Module is encountered) seamlessly in a VS debug 
session. The managed code is debugged in VS, the legacy COBOL is stepped 
through in the Fujitsu supplied debugger. (it includes features like 
Animation, breakpoints, watchpoints, all the things you expect from a modern 
debugger...) and it runs seamlessly with managed code where InterOP services 
provides the proxy for it.

But the important point is that legacy code is exactly that... "legacy". It 
has BEEN debugged and has been running in Production for a considerable time 
(often years) without problem. It doesn't NEED debugging and if it really 
did, then you'd move it back to the legacy platform and debug it there. 
That's what you have been doing since it was written; why is that suddenly a 
problem? (It actually isn't...)

Java, when properly written will equal or match COBOL when properly written, 
in both accuracy and performance. I know because I write both of them 
properly... :-) (Although I enjoy writing C# properly more than either of 
them.) I say it again:

It is programmers and programming which assure "enterprise
performance and reliability" NOT the languages they use...

(perhaps I should say it is not JUST programmers but the efforts of the 
entire IT team (Analysts, Network specialists, DBAs and even Management) 
that actually ensure performance and reliability, but either way it is not 
down to tools, it is down to people. The tools can help, but in the end it 
is the artist and not the paintbrush :-) (as a sometime poster here used to 
regularly remark).

Easy. Run it as is, unmanaged. (personally, I'd refactor it first and wrap

>>

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

Why I'm not working for anyone promoting such a compiler, or why I consider 
it a recipe for disaster :-)?

I HAVE worked for people who produce compilers and I have personally worked 
on maintaining 2 COBOL compilers, but it was a long time ago and I grew... 
:-)

Why I think simply re-compiling legacy COBOL for .NET is a recipe for 
disaster, is a matter of record, but quickly summing it up:

1. The Network needs Objects and Layers.
2. Legacy COBOL is NOT Objects and layers.
3. Legacy COBOL will not run well on the .Net Framework.

(Unless you do something to make it look like Objects and layers. I spent a 
couple of years writing tools that DO that and the results have been very 
well received. I did it because I needed to get my own stuff off COBOL and 
realised I couldn't possibly discard all of it (exactly as you mentioned 
above; there are business rules and computations that are buried in Legacy 
and it would be insane to discard them. So, they became Objects and run on 
.Net as first class citizens, playing nicely with the new objects written in 
the new languages.)

As Howard has pointed out, some companies will see Migration as a chance to 
rid themselves of Legacy and virtually start over, with newer languages, on 
a newer platform, or with a package solution. But other companies simply 
can't do that and need to "salvage" as much as possible. My company was in 
the latter group.

Java now just as decimal arithmetic exists on mainframes. C# handles 
decimals effortlessly.

>> OO COBOL is as foreign to most COBOL people as C++ or Assembler. You
>> may have seen posts here from COBOL guys bitterly bewailing that it
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> traditional procedural COBOL.

No, there isn't.

I thought there was for many years, but I realise now it was futile.

For OO COBOL to have made any difference it would need to have been embraced 
by the COBOL community 15 years ago. There would then be a wide OO COBOL 
base (now) and your arguments would stack up in practice instead of in 
theory.

But it wasn't. Most people understood it to be modular programming 
re-invented (it isn't of course, but no amount of showing why it isn't made 
any difference... in the end I gave up and just used it for myself.)

 Those of us who DID embrace it and tried to encourage others to do so 
received scorn (at best) and calumny and abuse (at worst) from the existing 
COBOL community. (To be fair, I for one may have tried too hard, but it is 
all academic now.) The OO COBOL boat sailed with nobody on it.

Fortunately, it doesn't matter.

Nobody NEEDS OO COBOL to use the .Net Framework. (You can simply use a .Net 
language...)

The only people who DO NEED OO COBOL are those who want to run LEGACY COBOL 
under .Net and then ONLY if you recognize that you NEED to objectify your 
legacy to get decent results...


> It may not win many academic prizes for
> programming model purity but it's a very productive way forward. The
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> saying, not just us).

Yes, there is "some truth" in your comment too :-)

I walked this very road myself going from a passionate supporter of 
procedural COBOL, through Modular Programming, to OO COBOL. Just as you 
suggest, I was seduced into believing that OO COBOL would get me into 
Objects. Eventually, it did and I was very happy to use it for over a 
decade.

But, in hindsight,  I wish I'd spent the time learning C# and Java. As soon 
as I did, I realized how lugubrious OO COBOL is and got away from it as 
quickly as possible. (Actually, I think the implementation of OO into COBOL 
is probably the single most impressive feat of software engineering that 
happened in the late 20th century. It was a stunning vision by a few people 
who were way ahead of their time and realized what was needed to keep COBOL 
viable. Unfortunately, the COBOL community didn't share that vision. The 
result is that by 2015, COBOL will probably no longer be viable as a major 
development language for commercial data processing. (It currently ranks 
30th and has around 2% of current development in the world.)

> that price.

it without paying money for it.

>

…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't want to try it. It would be a backward step for me. I am NOT 
committed to COBOL development and have moved away from it. Having 
successfully migrated, why would I go back? I maintain an interest in COBOL 
because I still have some legacy COBOL components and because some of my 
clients are still using COBOL or in the process of phasing it out. None of 
this requires a CLR generating COBOL compiler.

I have looked at the equivalent Fujitsu product in detail. It is a good 
product.

I have seen 2 videos on Visual COBOL and it is also a good product. (The 
second video, as reported here, was disappointing, to me at least, but the 
implementation is professional and excellent.)

But why would I use either of these products when I can use C#? If the aswer 
is:"In order to deal with your COBOL legacy." my response is: "I can do that 
very effectively with a few lines of code and InterOP services, thank you."

That is my whole point.

In a world of objects and layers, COBOL is NOT the best tool for the job.

Pretending it is so you can sell a compiler doesn't make it so, irrespective 
of how good the compiler is or what "new" features it provides.



>>
>> Here is the syllogism that sums up my argument against NetCOBOL for
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> describe.

That is an excellent question and a very fair one.

There IS a question about the whole relevance of COBOL  for the future, as 
we have been discussing.

But if you REALLY want to help people migrate their COBOL code (rather than 
just recompile it...)

1. Look at tools to encapsulate and refactor COBOL code into objects. 
Analyse source code and pull out PERFORMed subroutines, grabbing the data 
elements they use as well. Generate this into COM components that can run 
anywhere (from a Web Page or a desktop).

2. Provide automatic conversion from indexed files to Relational databases 
with proper processing of DATE types, REDEFINES, and OCCURS, producing a 
normalized RDB (minimal 2NF, 3NF where possible) AND producing a series of 
database maintenance objects (Data Access Layer - DAL) that can provide a 
separation layer (Objects and Layers) between the application programs and 
the data resource. This should all be generated automatically from existing 
COBOL source books.

3. Produce application programs to read the old indexed files and load them 
to the new normalized RDB. Because these are generated as COBOL source, it 
is pretty easy to insert hooks and filters manually into the DB load 
process, if required. (In practice, surprisingly little tailoring is usually 
required.)

4. Provide automatic refactoring of legacy code to utilize the DAL 
(replacing all indexed file access with invokes of DAL objects.) These 
generated DAL objects can be used by .Net Languages and/or by COBOL because 
they use the platform independent COM (Component Object Model) interface.

Or, you could just make me an offer for PRIMA, which already has 2 thru 4 of 
the above in place, and is working on the refactoring in 1.  :-)

Seriously, if you approach it from the point of view of what is actually 
needed to EFFECTIVELY move to .Net, it becomes apparent that DATA AND CODE 
migration are an integral part of it. It isn't just about recompiling. I 
guess it is a bit like decomposing the existing code, then rebuilding it 
with a Relational Database replacing all the indexed access, and everything 
converted to .Net Classes. The idea is to automate as much of that as 
possible. Fortunately, the nature of COBOL is such that the data resource is 
meticulously defined and therefore pretty easy to restructure as a 
normalized RDB.

Note that the above approach requires no middleware or "locking in" to some 
third party supplier. The data resource is moved to RDB (choice of SQL 
Server, MS Access, or PostgreSQL at the moment, with plans oto add DB2 
if/when we go to mainframe Migration) and the code that currently accesses 
it is automatically refactored to use a Data Acess Layer. (the advantages in 
doing this are huge; more than I can go into here.)
The client retains the code base just as they did before Migration, and the 
Migration process is accomplished using the client's existing staff with 
assistance from our toolset, so the local programmers understand how it all 
works.

So far, we have effected Migrations for sites using Fujitsu COBOL and PR1ME 
COBOL. When the pending one is complete in the New Year, I'll be looking for 
mainframe and Micro Focus sites that want to go to .Net...

While I would be first to admit that our approach isn't the only possible 
good one, it does address the whole area of making COBOL suitable for an 
Object environment. There have been no failures to date.

>
>>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

Pete

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: MicroFocus and Visual Cobol

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
