- **References:** `<bbdd0e70-be5a-48c0-9846-60e3c8d3481f@m35g2000vbn.googlegroups.com> <8mc838Fg36U1@mid.individual.net> <346caeeb-25b1-4f3b-a279-34d3b5e8da03@s5g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <8mgho7Fk16U1@mid.individual.net> <87ec1cac-282b-406c-b367-930fc7bfb7ff@l24g2000vby.googlegroups.com> <8mmnknFj4U1@mid.individual.net> <ad831508-7ec4-413a-8117-554efcb4c2a4@z19g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`

```
MarkW wrote:
> On Dec 13, 2:07 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> just like any other .NET component written in C# or VB.

So are Projects containing InterOP. I have a number of  Managed projects 
which use COBOL components running under InterOP services. They are deployed 
exactly as projects which DON'T contain InterOP services. A COM component is 
a COM component. It has a standard interface and whether it is managed or 
unmanaged, it is viewed through that interface by anything that wants to use 
it.



 That reduces
> one of the complaints we often hear from IT organizations - "COBOL is
> different. I know how to manage .NET/JVM applications but I don't
> understand these "strange" formats."

The "strange formats" don't go away because something is deployed to .Net.


There are also tools available
> for monitoring and tuning performance that "just work" for COBOL
> on .NET/JVM that don't support native formats.

The primary tool for tuning performance is a debugger. I covered that 
already. It isn't a problem for managed or unmanaged code.
>
>> Microsoft realised at the time they designed .Net that there would
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>> True but Interop introduces complexity
I disagree. It is just another Class. In the early incarnations of .Net 
where it had to be implemented by PINVOKE, it was tedious. But the later 
versions of the Framework have a much more seamless integration and mostly 
it is transparent.

>and can cause performance
> hits (YMMV).

Never in my experience. But as you say, experiences can differ...

>The goal should be to reduce complexity and layers
> between application logic and execution paths as that reduces costs
> ultimately.

At this point we have to diverge. I agree with reducing complexity but 
layers are the absolute lifeblood of object oriented systems. COBOL people 
generally don't see it because the COBOL approach is for a single program to 
manage its own presentation and data access. These are usually inextricably 
entangled with Business logic. But once the Business Logic is encapsulated 
into objects, there is a very good case for separating the Presentation and 
Data Access Layers out from it. (PRIMA's tools automatically create a Data 
Access Layer comprised of objects that manage the Database, and modify the 
existing codebase to use that layer, but that is really just a step in the 
right direction. Currently, we don't do anything about the Presentation 
Layer during Migration, although I am looking at that currently.)

 Objects communicate with each other through interfaces anyway, so it makes 
sense to encapsulate the functionality in them into "areas", "tiers" or 
"layers" where objects that are performing a similar function become 
components in that layer. A multi-tiered approach has HUGE benefits for 
maintaining systems. If you like, the "granularity" of the system is 
smaller, so you can swap components in and out without causing any knock on 
effects. Layers can be independently tuned and experimented with with no 
effect on the business logic, the business logic can be changed with no 
effect on other layers, and if the change DOES require something added into 
Presentation and Data Access Layers, the changes are in an encapsulated 
component that cannot effect other processing.  The concept of layering 
systems is  generally foreign to COBOL, but it is nonetheless, an extremely 
good way to build systems. Object Oriented systems lend themselves to it 
very well and that is one of the reasons why legacy code should be 
"objectified" before moving it to .Net.

If you consider a CICS or IMS/DC system on IBM mainframes, they lend 
themselves to this approach because the Business logic is already separated 
from the presentation by a screen buffer (Map, in CICS). This is the same 
principle as the interfaces I mentioned above. You can change a CICS BMS Map 
and it MAY have no effect on the Business Logic at all. The Business Logic 
populates a screen buffer (including attributes that affect the display) and 
that is all the Business Logic ever sees. It Neither knows nor cares about 
the sus=bsystem that will present the data to the User. Layered Object 
systems work in very much the same way.

Layers and Objects. The Mantra of the Network... :-)

>
>> The REAL question is simply: "Do I want to move off COBOL?"
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> on that subject as you can probably imagine, we don't agree!

Yes, I respect your point of view on that but it is hardly unbiased... :-)

Time will tell.

At the moment, the trend is against you...

Fewer and fewer people with a traditional or emotional attachment to COBOL 
are still employed.  Although you may be able to show graduates that COBOL 
CAN work in the Brave New World, they will soon realise that it is tedious 
to do it in COBOL. (I know, I've been there... :-))

You CAN start a fire by rubbing sticks together, you COULD ride a horse to 
work, you might swim or row across a river instead of taking the bridge or 
the ferry, but most of us don't have the luxury of unlimited time and most 
of us use power tools instead of a saw or gimlet.

COBOL was a brilliantly conceived language that arose out of a need for 
cross platform compatibility and a more intuitive approach than AND, OR or 
XOR on binary digits... It served us well for half a century and I 
personally made a very good living out of it, but it has been overtaken by 
events and I believe it is time to let it go gracefully.

I know you won't agree and I wish you well in your endeavours. Like I said, 
time will tell.
>
>>> How well can the code be optimized or how good can the
…[61 more quoted lines elided]…
> we've seen the failures as well.

No Mark, I'm not advocating rewriting code that "seems to be working well". 
On the contrary, I'm all for refactoring and extending the life of existing 
COBOL code. If you can do it effectively, it buys you a good deal of time, 
during which your staff can be trained in the new Environment and languages. 
My objection is that it needs to be tweaked before Migration, I don't see a 
place for CONTINUING COBOL development., and there is no need to buy an 
expensive CLR generating COBOL compiler to do it, UNLESS you intend to 
continue developing in COBOL. I believe that is not a viable long term 
option, but I accept there will be a number of people who disagree.  (Please 
have a look at: http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21
 which has sample code showing how legacy can be converted to objects with 
just a few lines of code... the site has registered over 4500 unique 
visitors since I wrote it over a year ago. Several hundred have downloaded 
the free tool and about a hundred have downloaded the source code in COBOL 
and C#. I honestly don't know how many PRIMA clients have been influenced by 
the Web Site but I should think most of them have at least looked at it.

>
>> Java, when properly written will equal or match COBOL when properly
…[83 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Very sound. I agree wholeheartedly. It is quite wrong to assume that all 
corporate applications need the same solution or to do it all with COBOL, 
because 'we've always done it that way'.

>>
>>
…[72 more quoted lines elided]…
> Micro Focus

Thanks, I enjoyed your post and your views. Hope it works out for you.

Pete.
```

As far as money goes, I think anybody whose sole product is a COBOL compiler 
is on a serious hiding to nothing. Fujitsu can survive :-) (They sell 
everything from heat pumps to COBOL compilers and could go out of COBOL and 
never miss it. Besides, their resources are enormous and they are a much 
larger company than most people would think.) They have divested their COBOL 
marketing (at least in North America) to Alchemy, who claim to have backers 
with deep pockets, but I should think even the deepest of pockets would 
become anxious as new sites for COBOL dry up. Micro Focus have market 
leadership in the mainframe Network COBOL arena and they are a good company 
with a good product. (Visual COBOL is a good implementation; my objection to 
it is that it is irrelevant, as you don't need a CLR generating COBOL 
compiler to move your COBOL applications to .Net, and if you do do this it 
simply degrades .Net, unless you objectify your legacy first. None of the 
companies advocating the use of CLR generating compilers have addressed this 
question. The idea is: "Buy our CLR compiler, recompile and everything runs 
in .Net and you are migrated." I find that annoying. Having actually done 
the exercise I can assure everybody without equivocation that there is FAR 
more to it than that.)

Micro Focus have  established a reputation and are generally well thought of 
by mainframe sites who have been traditional Fortress COBOL shops. But the 
fortresses are falling. Unless MF diversify into other areas their future is 
not bright. I think we are seeing some hintsof this in the video with the 
applcation of COBOL to mobile phones and other devices. The problem with 
that is that it must then compete with languages which are much quicker to 
write and DESIGNED for dealing with Objects.

Never mind. I could be wrong. Time will tell.
>
> The mind boggles how much they will want for a upgrade from the other

Sunday... gotta go :-)

<a id="t-73785fbf07"></a>
## Thread: Software protection — 8 msgs

### Software protection

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-09T13:16:36+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8malf5Fc0iU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
PRIMA is about to release a new software protection product which will 
guarantee Developers no piracy of their packages. (Pirate (non-validated) 
copies of a package simply won't run.)

It is called "Remote Application Validation" (RAV)

RAV doesn't use serial numbers or product activation codes, so there is no 
chance of people obtaining "cracked" copies of these from hacker web sites.

RAV is implemented by adding a single COM component to your application, and 
a web service to your web server. (If you don't have a web server (typically 
it would be running your company's web pages) we can supply one for you 
either in the cloud or at an ISP location, for around $US300 per year. Both 
of these components are tailored by PRIMA specifically for you as a 
Developer. You can protect as many packages as you like from the same web 
server and you can run as many different packages on client sites as you 
like, once you are set up. RAV has no impact on your Application 
functionality (it has no visible user interface and dos not interact wth 
Users of your package); it simply tells you what level of access is 
appropriate and what run limitations are in place (if any), and whether any 
of these rules have been exceeded. What you do after that is over to you.

It allows  different levels of access to the same software and includes run 
limitation by number of executions or a time period (or both) for DEMO 
versions of your package.

RAV recognises whether your package is being leased or is owned outright. If 
it is being leased, you can stop execution of it at any time without access 
to the client site.

It was written in response to a request from a client using TrialPro, which 
is now defunct. Anyone who has TrialPro will know what problems this 
software addresses, however it does it in a completely different way from 
TrialPro and is much more flexible and powerful.

The RAV system has currently been tried with packages written in C# and VB 
and works very well, but I'd like to try it with a completely "strange" (not 
my own) package, written, ideally, in COBOL.

If anyone here would be prepared to act as a Beta tester (and thereby get a 
free copy of the final product) please contact me. Here is the ideal profile 
I'm looking for, but if you don't quite match, but are still interested, 
please contact me anyway...

1. You have a software package which you have made a significant investment 
in.
2.  People can buy the package or lease it.
3.  You generally deploy it using a site licence (All machines at the client 
site can run it). If your package is deployed to a server at the client 
site,  RAV can optionally allow a "staggered" site licence (LOW = say, 3 
machines can use the package concurrently, MEDIUM = 10 machines can use the 
package concurrently, HIGH = 20 machines can use the package concurrently, 
ENTERPRISE = unlimited concurrent users (limited only by your LAN 
hardware/software. The numbers shown are suggestions only.).
4. RAV does not currently provide protection to an individual client machine 
(it is to a client site). This saves a great deal of effort registering 
client machines, however, if protection down to this level was absolutely 
required, it can be fairly easily extended to do so, and I'd be interested 
to talk to anyone requiring it.

RAV is targeted to small independent software developers and software 
houses, for whom piracy can be the difference between solvency and 
bankruptcy. It will be priced so people can afford to have it. I'll be 
integrating it into PRIMA's existing product range in the New Year.

Pete.
```

```
Binyamin Dissen wrote:
> On Thu, 9 Dec 2010 13:16:36 +1300 "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> program?

Nope. The program will "tolerate" a Developer configured consecutive number 
of days without access to the server. (There is a default which I'm not 
going to mention here). It was pretty obvious during design that the 
Internet could be down for a number of valid reasons and it would be 
completely wrong to disable the software. After that it tells you to contact 
the Developer. Remember, this only applies to people who are leasing the 
package; if you bought it outright, it doesn't use the web service. (In 
fact, even if you are leasing it, it doesn't use the web service every time 
it is started; it decides when it needs to connect..)

Thanks for your thought, Binyamin. This is exactly the kind of feedback I 
was looking for. I may think I have everything covered, but unless other 
eyes look at it, I can't know for sure.

Cheers,

Pete.

## Thread: our Christmas staff dinner... — 1 msgs

### our Christmas staff dinner...

- **From:** Kerry Liles <"kerry.liles[minus_the_spam]"@gmail.com>

## Thread: COBOL and a whole lot more! — 39 msgs

### COBOL and a whole lot more!

- **From:** seasoned_geek <roland@logikalsolutions.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-11T09:23:43-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1f717040-19fe-4fe6-9995-5a2fc7536cc6@i18g2000yqn.googlegroups.com>`

```
If you are new to COBOL, or just looking to learn about COBOL on a
different platform, or want to branch out from COBOL to a new
language, this book is for you.

http://www.theminimumyouneedtoknow.com/app_book.html

The same application developed over and over again using different
languages and different data storage methods.  So, if you know one of
the covered languages, you can quickly come up to speed with another
of the covered languages.

Here is what one college student said after plunking down their hard
earned money to buy it.

"Amazing.  This is a truly astonishing work and a MUST for everybody
who is starting with application developing, be it on VMS or not (I
can not tell for others as I am starting myself). It is not a book
where you will find many theories about abstract topics but one
displaying everything you need to know by means of examples,
explaining each example's important parts in-depth. You really feel
the experience of the author throughout the text! This is a book I'd
try to take with me if my house was burning down."
Thierry Dussuet, OpenVMS Student
```

```
No,  You cannot get a free video tutorial on OpenVMS, DCL, RMS indexed
files, print and batch queue management, FMS (the documentation for
FMS isn't even put out by HP anymore), CMS (Code Management System),
MMS (Module Management System), Oracle RDB, OpenVMS Mail, DEC BASIC,
VAX FORTRAN, DEC C/C++, DEC COBOL.  You might be able to find one for
MySQL, which is also covered, but it won't explain how to use MySQL on
that platform, just some generic PC platform which won't be even close
for this target.  And while you are at it, search for that tutorial
which shows you how to translate a FORTRAN program into COBOL, a BASIC
program into COBOL, a C program into COBOL, and COBOL back into each
of those languages.

There is a reason OpenVMS Engineering chose to pass this book out at a
Technical Boot Camp and to create training seminars around it.  There
is nothing more complete out there, from any publisher.

But, there will always be those who believe all others should work for
free...except themselves of course.

```
seasoned_geek wrote:
> No,  You cannot get a free video tutorial on OpenVMS, DCL, RMS indexed
> files, print and batch queue management, FMS (the documentation for
> FMS isn't even put out by HP anymore), CMS (Code Management System),
> MMS (Module Management System), Oracle RDB, OpenVMS Mail, DEC BASIC,
> VAX FORTRAN, DEC C/C++, DEC COBOL.

Well, I can't get a free video for Bendix Assembler either.

The only remotely relevant topic in the above is Oracle RDBMS, for which 
there are numerous video tutorials on the Internet. (a search for "free 
oracle video tutorials" yielded over 40,000,000 results...)

I worked on DEC equipment when it was PDP (and, later, on VAX...) It was 
good. So were NCR computers. And Burroughs... And Honeywell...

But they simply failed to remain relevant to a changing marketplace and are 
no more.

HP own what's left of DEC "by accident" because they acquired Compaq.

The book you are talking about may have relevance for some computer 
historians, but (generally) not for Developers trying to make a living in 
2011.

> You might be able to find one for
> MySQL, which is also covered, but it won't explain how to use MySQL on
> that platform, just some generic PC platform which won't be even close
> for this target.

MySQL was absorbed by Oracle around a year ago. As for using MySQL on "that 
platform", why would it be  any more difficult than using it on any other 
platform? The whole point about having a Relational model that is consistent 
is that you CAN use it across platforms. I have had considerable experience 
with using different RDBMS (Including Oracle and MySQL) on different 
platforms, and yes, there are often minor differences in SQL implementations 
and syntax but for the most part it isn't hard to replace one RDBMS with 
another. And I have never had a case where the information needed to do so 
could not be obtained for free from the Internet.


>And while you are at it, search for that tutorial
> which shows you how to translate a FORTRAN program into COBOL, a BASIC
> program into COBOL, a C program into COBOL, and COBOL back into each
> of those languages.

And the one that translates Latin into Sanskrit, Assyrian into Aramaic, and 
Babylonian cuneiform into Egyptian hieroglyphics...?

It's like telling a teacher of English as a second language in the 21st 
century, to buy a book on the languages of  pre-Indo-European  Asia., 
because it MIGHT have something of use in it, and it is about languages...

I'm not questioning the value of the book (I haven't read it...) I'm simply 
questioning who it is for.

In your original post you said it was for:

"If you are new to COBOL, or just looking to learn about COBOL on a
different platform, or want to branch out from COBOL to a new
language, this book is for you."

You didn't mention it is aimed at VMS students. Given that HP's OpenVMS has 
less than .01 % of the World's OS Market, has shadows over it even within HP 
(who are more focused on  HP UX as a long term product), and is facing a 
constant battle for survival against Linux and UNIX-like alternatives, it 
seems hard to see this book as being anything other than for a very narrow 
niche market.




>
> There is a reason OpenVMS Engineering chose to pass this book out at a
> Technical Boot Camp and to create training seminars around it.

(They got it at a good price?)

>There
> is nothing more complete out there, from any publisher.

Why am I not surprised?

>
> But, there will always be those who believe all others should work for
> free...except themselves of course.

No, you miss my point. I have invested a considerable sum of my own money 
into training, books, and videos. I certainly don't expect people to work 
for nothing and if you talk to anyone who has worked for me they will tell 
you I am a generous employer. (I certainly have no trouble getting people to 
work for/with me and retaining them when I  need to.)

I mention the fact that tutorials and videos on the Internet are free 
because it is relevant, not because I think they SHOULD be. I taught myself 
C# almost entirely from such videos, but if you were to look at my 
bookshelves you will find a number of publications there about this 
language.

The nature of information and the dissemination of it has changed radically 
in the last 30 years. That doesn't mean that books are obsolete, but it does 
mean that the reasons we have for referencing them bear scrutiny. There was 
a time when, if you wanted to know something, you went to a library or 
looked it up in a book. While we still do that, it is no longer our first 
recourse and much of the time we can solve our problem from sources that are 
immediate, free, and already available without an expensive trip to the 
bookstore.

As noted above, the problem here is where a $100 book on "COBOL and a whole 
lot more!" fits into meeting the needs of a 21st century computer 
programmer. If it was a $30 book, people could quite reasonably take a punt, 
and no harm is done if it doesn't fit, but at $100, it requires some 
consideration. Can I go to Amazon and read some reviews or a synopsis, for 
example?

Having had this discussion I am convinced there is no need for me to buy 
this book, but I would hesitate tbefore recommending it to a young mainframe 
programmer who was going to be using COBOL.

Nevertheless, I wish you well with it.

Pete.
```

On Dec 23, 9:05 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
> The only remotely relevant topic in the above is Oracle RDBMS, for which
> there are numerous video tutorials on the Internet. (a search for "free
> oracle video tutorials" yielded over 40,000,000 results...)

Not one of which is for RDB.  Those are all for the buggy Oracle
database running on Windows and other platforms.  RDB is the only high
availability fault tolerant database able to completely utilize a
cluster.  It was developed by DEC and purchased by Oracle.  Even
Oracle's be-all-end-all RAC product does not achieve the level of
fault tolerance of RDB as is evident by the gaggle of lawyers squaring
off from  CAT and several other companies.

>
> I worked on DEC equipment when it was PDP (and, later, on VAX...) It was
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I humbly suggest you get some actual information about the computer
industry before making such posts.  OpenVMS runs 80% of the steel
mills and 90% of the paper mills in the world.  It is currently the
center of most high availability trading engines.  The world's most
worthless form of Unix, HP-UX, was recently thrown out of the primary
stock exchange in China to be replaced by a modern system running
OpenVMS.

http://www.slideshare.net/Itaniumalliance/shanghai-stock-exchangesuccessstory
http://features.techworld.com/operating-systems/1001/openvms-survives-and-thrives/

The week following the Beijing Olympics, construction began on the
largest and most modern steel mill anywhere in the world.  They came
to the U.S. to get a control system written in DEC FORTRAN ported to
the Itamium OpenVMS platform and expanded for the actual number of
rails they would have in simultaneous operation.  I know, I helped
design the upgrades.

I won't tell you just how many nuclear plants its operating, or point
out that it is the backbone for the Patriot Missile System or how many
thousand ManMan installations are still out there operating profitable
companies.  I won't bother telling you that BlockBuster still runs
OpenVMS for its video rental system or that every concert TicketMaster
sells is sold and printed via OpenVMS.


> > You might be able to find one for
> > MySQL, which is also covered, but it won't explain how to use MySQL on
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> could not be obtained for free from the Internet.

Well, obviously you don't care about quality or accuracy, because you
are displaying quite a lack of knowledge.

Oracle purchased InnoDB which was the OpenSource relational engine
bundled with MySQL on SOME platforms.  If you knew anything at all
about MySQL you would know that it's a limited and somewhat buggy
wrapper around a multitude of storage systems.  The default storage
system for MySQL isn't relational at all, but its own proprietary
indexed file system which has a lot of issues, especially when it gets
over to OpenVMS which already has an amazing indexed file system with
cluster wide availability and journaling.  Of all the platforms it
ships on, InnoDB was only the DEFAULT storage format on OpenVMS.
Installation and configuration of MySQL is dramatically different on
OpenVMS, as it is on the IBM mainframe running the new Z/OS than it is
for hokey windows boxes or cobbled together Linux systems.

Since your experience was migrating between Oracle and MySQL, you had
the experience of migrating between two feeble databases with limited
data storage types.  Neither of them support a quad-word datatype, or
a host of other datatypes which are natively supported by RDB and
OpenVMS and required for the industries they support.  You also must
have some massively dated porting experience, pre-stored procedure
era, because Oracle went out of its way with PL/SQL to make it non-
portable to any other database.

>
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> niche market.

You really, really, really, need to get some credible stats and some
current information.  While HP's UX division has been trying for a
while to kill off OpenVMS, the industries which require it (and which
all modern civilization is based on) have thrown HP-UX out on its ass
along with Windows.

http://news.cnet.com/8301-13846_3-10036286-62.html

In 2003, HP claimed the OpenVMS business was growing 10-12% per year.

The great line: "While most enterprise-class IT environments measure
uptime in days, weeks, or months, customers using OpenVMS environments
regularly characterize uptime in terms of years."

http://www.bigurllive.info/uptime.html
http://thedailyreviewer.com/compsys/view/re-longest-uptime-105320504


>
> As noted above, the problem here is where a $100 book on "COBOL and a whole
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> example?

Those with ethics do not do business with Amazon.  You can read all
you want on Barnes & Noble, and many other places, not to mention at
the site for the series.  http://www.theminimumyouneedtoknow.com

Nevertheless, I wish you well in your future endeavors.

```
On Dec 28, 6:36 pm, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
> If you and 'seasoned geek' had sufficient reading skills you should
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
 Oh correction:
>> Nothing's that entrenched.
> Least of all OpenVMS.

Then came all the boilerplate stuff.


>
> This runs on 'legacy' Itanium as well as current architectures from
> Intel and AMD.

Itanium was legacy before it was made.

>
> It would not surprise me to find HP actively trying to move OpenVMS
> and Tandem Nonstop customers to HP XC.
>

Follow your history.  Intel hasn't designed a chip since the x86.
Intel was caught red-handed thieving vast quantities of Alpha tech
while it was partnered with HP trying to create a "me too" 64-bit
processor.  At the end of the 64-bit processor life span, they
introduce Itanic and wonder why nobody will buy a chip from two
companies that have never been able to design a worthwhile chip.  None
of their installed base buys.  What do they do?  They immediately stop
production of Alpha, which had at least 5 more years worth of
enhancements on the table at the time, and force OpenVMS onto the
worst chip the industry has ever seen just to generate sales.  Then
they report the chip sales to their installed base, which refused to
move, but leave out the "minor details" those sales were all forced
OpenVMS customers.  Given that HP-UX is quite possibly the worst Unix
distro in the history of man (yes, even including Ultrix), Itanic
seems like it should have been a match made in heaven.

Fast forward a few years:

Even the drunkest heroin junky on the planet has to realize Itanic is
a massively failed processor.  Had it been 128-bit, it might have had
a reason to exist.  AMD eclipsed Intel a loooong time ago, but, HP is
literally a partner in crime with Intel, so they can't really get
behind using one of AMD's high end chips.

Guess what's going to happen?

This new round of stuff they are trying to sell won't sell.  Once
again HP will outright shaft the OpenVMS installed base by dropping
all support for the processor they are on and forcing them to either
upgrade to Itanic-2 (let's face it, if either Intel or HP had a hand
in the design, the chip is a shipwreck before it leaves the dock), or
bail on the platform, or, buy the Charon-VAX-ALPHA-Itanium emulator
stuff.  (For those who don't know, there are still a lot of companies
running PDP stuff on emulators.)

AMD has created some incredible non-x86 chips.  A port to any of them
would be a great option for adding another 15-20 years to OpenVMS.

HP has to keep OpenVMS around because nobody will buy their products
otherwise.  Take a good look at their SEC filings.  HP-UX ain't a cash
cow.  Now that there is massive competition for ink-jet carts, the
cash cow has died.  Lexmark and Kodak now sell printers with NEW black
ink carts costing $10 retail.  Gone are the days of HP charging $45
for black ink and your first born child for a color cart.  They quit
doing their own engineering for laptops and desktops some years ago,
instead relying on Intel to provide the specs.  Now they have me-too
PCs competing with Dell and Gateway on price instead of carving a path
with their own functionality.
```

I base the 30 year estimate on the fact that it has been roughly 30
years since the last PDP was made by DEC and the PDP emulate still has
quite a bit of sales.  The emulators cannot handle everything, the x86
based CPUs simply aren't fit for much beyond pocket calculators, both
the VAX and the Alpha were a lot more processor than the x86 family
could ever hope to be.

code.  When Intel was caught red-handed engaging in corporate and
intellectual property theft, part of the settlement was that they had
to change the response register (along with a lot of other things
which made sure the Itanic would be a cripple coming out the
door...but their engineers couldn't see that).  Prior to the Itanic
all CPUs VMS had been ported to sent results back in R0.  A few other
"standard register uses" aren't the same on the Itanic.

```
> Funny, I would have said comp.lang.cobol would've been and ideal place to 
> find them.
> 
> Anyone else find the irony of a bunch of cobol hacks proclaiming the
> death of VMS being too much to bear.

Your analogy is invalid. VMS was never a popular platform. COBOL has been
around since the 1950s and has more linecount in production code than all
other languages combined. COBOL has active standardization groups. Work is
being done on updating the language and people are spending money on that
and on updating optimizing compilers. New COBOL code is still being
written. I know of one recent deployment that cost about 500 million USD
for a COBOL conversion including millions of lines of new code.

VMS is not in production and hasn't been for years. There's nothing new in
VMS nor in the apps that used to run on it. It's an obsolete platform.

What's too much to bear is how out of touch you PC-weenies are. You only
know about the small machines like PCs and minis so you simply don't
realize COBOL is still going today. Just because COBOL isn't a language for
toy computers doesn't mean it's obsolete.

You would have been better off comparing VAX to IBM System/360 but you
still would have been on the losing end. Even those old clunkers can run
circles around the biggest baddest VAX ever made.

> Sadly, the parallel-universe that is academia can distort one's
> perception of reality.

Indisputably, but what's the connection to this thread? COBOL hasn't been
used in academia since the days of VAX machines. But COBOL is being
used in production, in new code, even today and vendors and customers are
still spending money on it.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL and a whole lot more!

_(thread depth: 17)_

- **From:** seasoned_geek <roland@logikalsolutions.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-29T13:34:26-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49fe9537-433d-4a7d-ba8c-17934d956259@o14g2000yqe.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<ifdqh0$47l$1@speranza.aioe.org> <3FIO7R0T40541.8315625@reece.net.au> <a727dad1-8dd2-49d9-86cf-a5ce2f7e6f77@p8g2000vbs.googlegroups.com> <8o1ekvFc0sU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Dec 29, 12:57 pm, billg...@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon) wrote:
>
> Historical data for IBM stock is readily available.  A quick look
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

And that's why teacher tenure has got to be ended for the good of
all.  They don't know how to do research, nor how tainted their data
sources are.  I personally worked with 3 consultants who formed some
temporary investment company that actually purchased IBM shares for
$25 and unloaded them a short time later for more than double their
money.  This was back before the Internet when you had to have a lot
of money to even open a brokerage account.  I certainly did not have
one.

Please allow me to explain to you what you are looking at when you use
those free historical sources.  You are looking at something which
periodically saved the CLS, then did a sort on it.  CLS is the
Consolidated Last Sale.  This is a weighted average over span of time
designed to both inform all of the stock exchanges and to smooth
market trading.  I don't remember the formula (if I ever knew it in
its entirety) but odd-lots and small-rounds rarely make it into the
CLS of a high volume issue.  For the longest time, the only sale which
could be printed to tape (sent to the primary stock exchange for
inclusion in the consolidated quote) was a trade which actually
occurred on the trading floor of a licensed stock exchange.  Orders
which were matched in a broker's office or on an ECN were not eligible
for inclusion.  Regulations have changed some over the years, but
those changes started in the late 1990s.

There used to be more stock exchanges than this in the U.S.
http://www.cftech.com/BrainBank/FINANCE/USStockExchs.html

The reporting lags caused by communications of each time period is
what gave rise to the CLS as a need for smoothing statistical
outliers.

The simple fact is, we don't have a central clearing agency in this
country.  While not all trades are not required to print to tape, all
trades are required to go through clearing.  The only way to really
know what the day's high and low were would be to gather information
about every trade from every clearing company in the world and sort
them.

Smoothing is required because the difference between the bid and the
ask at any given exchange (known as the spread) can become quite wide,
especially with automated trading rules.  When a market order comes
(MKT) it has to automatically execute against Top of Book (TOB).  If
the local CTOB (Consolidated TOB) is better than the local TOB (LTOB)
the MKT has to be executed against specialist's inventory at CTOB
price, or be laid off to that market...which...given communications
lag times opens another can of worms.

An Odd-lot, is any potential trade consisting of less than a round-lot
quantity.  Many issues have a 100 share round-lot, but some issues,
like the Berkshire stuff, have 10 share round-lots.  There could even
be some which have 10000 share rounds, I don't remember.  Odd-lot
traders are a completely different world.  I never understood the odd-
lot way of life.  I never understood how a specialist could kick your
25 share trade into that system, or split your 50045 share trade into
a 50000 share trade they handled and 45 which went through they odd-
lot way of life.  I do know that back then you could get dramatically
different prices for the round-lot and odd-lot portions of your trades
unless you paid extra money to put an AON qualifier on your order.
All-Or-None meant the specialist receiving the order had to fill the
entire order in one trade or reject the order.  This also had the
benefit of protecting you from multiple trade fees (not as big an
issue with 7.95 trades as it was with $35+ trading fees).  If you sent
in an order to sell 500,000 shares of some issue at a fixed price and
became the CTOB, your order could be matched against 100 orders of
5,000 shares each and each match be a separate trade/clearing fee in
the eyes of your broker.

Sorry, it's been a long time since I had to remember that life.  All I
can tell you is that, while it is more difficult now, just about
anyone can buy any issue at any price and it won't show up in your
research.  Where in your research does it indicate the sale price when
an executive with a stock option strike price, or writ-20 executed a
trade?  Both trades occurred, well outside what will be listed for
daily high/low, but, odds are they never got printed to tape.  A print
like that would reek havoc emptying out specialist books on all
exchanges.


I can tell you from personal experience that it wasn't the Digital
name, but the quality of OpenVMS and clustering which sold the VAX. I
was even at a local DECUS (Chug-a-Lug) meeting where Ken Olsen showed
up fronting for his new company and said the exact same thing...it
wasn't the VAX, but VMS which sold the package.
```

```
In case Indeed.com doesn't work for you for some reason, try searching
job openings on Monster.

http://jobsearch.monster.com/PowerSearch.aspx?q=VAX%20COBOL&tm=60&rad=20&rad_units=miles


After all, very few of today's college kids will ever succeed hanging
out a shingle as an IC.  Many simply want to get a decent paying job
at a really big company with 401K matching.  The core business systems
tend to survive the first few rounds of layoffs in a downturn.

Oddly enough the JustCobolJobs page seems to match a lot of jobs which
don't appear to list COBOL...then again it tosses you to a different
site.

http://js.justtechjobs.com/JSSearchResults.asp?PAGE=1

Put your feet in the shoes of a student on their second year of
college getting an IT degree.  While the world maybe hyping Web
design, Java, php, and a boat load of other trendy things, that same
world leaves off the part about how students graduating with those
skills will spend their entire lives competing against $10/day labor
from third world countries.

What IT skills ensure you have a chance at becoming very highly
compensated and layoff immune?  Whatever skills are required for the
care and feeding of the Cash Cow.  Sometimes that's COBOL on OpenVMS,
other times it is BASIC, or FORTRAN on the same platform.

I know of one particular company which is now embarking on its fourth
attempt to replace a Cash Cow system originally written in DEC BASIC
with yet another be-all-end-all-ERP-product-sold-by-Gartner-this-
week.  So far, all of those products have failed spectacularly.

So, if you are just starting out, you need to get the skills to get
your foot in the door.  After you have a few years working with one of
the languages on that platform supporting a Cash Cow, you can poke
around and find another company with a Cash Cow on the same platform,
if it happens to be a different language, not a problem, you've got a
book which will let you quickly jump.

Here is some free career advance:

If there is a "free training video" on-line, or you can plunk down $20
for a copy of "Teach Yourself How to Be Totally Useless in 21 Days or
Less" for the skill set, you don't _ever_ want to consider entering
that market.  It will always be awash with $10/day or less labor and
you will always be "this" far from being replaced with someone who
doesn't speak your language.
```

<a id="t-5b7111b30f"></a>
## Thread: inline read loop — 3 msgs

### inline read loop

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-17T16:54:34-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4D0B95CA.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
My recent message about an "inline perform" without an UNTIL clause got me
thinking.  Wouldn't it be nice to have a READ that *has* an UNTIL?

Current read loop:
PERFORM UNTIL END-PURGE            
    READ TXFRHST-FILE NEXT RECORD  
    AT END                         
        SET END-PURGE TO TRUE      
    NOT AT END                     
        PERFORM 0510-DELETE-IF-OLD 
    END-READ                       
END-PERFORM                        

How about this instead?

READ TXFRHST-FILE NEXT RECORD 
     UNTIL AT END
NOT AT END                    
    PERFORM 0510-DELETE-IF-OLD
END-READ                      

Notice the addition of "UNTIL AT END".  So it would read each record,
performing the code in the "NOT AT END" clause, and then quit once we've
read and processed the last record.

No new keywords or, really, even new concepts.  Just makes the code more
compact and perhaps more obvious, once you get used to it.  No checking for
"at end" essentially twice.  And yes, I do realize you can have END-PURGE
defined as the 88 level name for the FILE STATUS for the file and do this
instead.

PERFORM UNTIL END-PURGE            
    READ TXFRHST-FILE NEXT RECORD  
    NOT AT END                     
        PERFORM 0510-DELETE-IF-OLD 
    END-READ                       
END-PERFORM                        

That doesn't even work for a RETURN from SORT statement, though, because the
FILE STATUS clause is not allowed.

RETURN TXFRSRT-FILE INTO TXFRHST-REC    
       UNTIL AT END                              
NOT AT END                              
    WRITE TXFRHST-REC                   
    INVALID KEY                         
        PERFORM 20XX-TXFRHST-INVALID-KEY
    END-WRITE                           
END-RETURN                              

Some inspiration(?) came from the Ruby programming language, which has
something like this:

txfrHstFile.each do |txfrHstRec|
    deleteIfOld(txfrHstRec)
end

Anyway, just felt like starting a new argument.  I have no expectation that
this would ever become part of any COBOL standard.  Just wasting time on a
Friday evening...

Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: inline read loop

- **From:** Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 2010-12-18T03:12:53-08:00

- **Message-ID:** `<0a4f38fc-cded-4061-8d8a-c60d55c9ecf6@y3g2000vbm.googlegroups.com>`

```

#### ↳ Re: inline read loop

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-19T01:44:14+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8n3okuFkihU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4D0B95CA.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
Frank Swarbrick wrote:
> My recent message about an "inline perform" without an UNTIL clause
> got me thinking.  Wouldn't it be nice to have a READ that *has* an
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> we've read and processed the last record.

No, it wouldn't.

You are expecting it to behave how you WANT it to behave, and not do what it 
actually says.

If there was such a construct, and it did what it says, it would read until 
end of file. (That means it would simply get every record, and your NOT AT 
END would never be raised...)

Interestingly, there is such a construct in C# which reads ALL records in a 
file into a result set that is a single string. Sometimes this can be very 
useful.

(For a line sequential text file, for instance, you can load the entire file 
to memory with a single "read" and then search it much more quickly than if 
you read and individually searched each record. I put "read" in quotes 
because it is done with a stream reader and it isn't a READ as understood in 
COBOL.)

The READ construct currently implemented in COBOL pauses after each record 
(thereby enabling AT END and NOT AT END to be evaluated) because it has only 
been told to get a single record.

If the construct you are proposing were to be impleneted, it would be 
impossible to tell syntactically which behaviour was required, the one you 
describe, or the one I describe.

Perhaps that's why it hasn't been implemented?

>
> No new keywords or, really, even new concepts.  Just makes the code
> more compact and perhaps more obvious, once you get used to it.

Or more confusing and ambiguous... :-)


>No
> checking for "at end" essentially twice.

There's really no need to check for AT END twice with the existing syntax...
IF NOT END-PURGE
   READ TXFRHST-FILE NEXT RECORD
       AT END
             SET END-PURGE TO TRUE
             CLOSE TXFRHST-FILE
       NOT AT END
              PERFORM 0510-DELETE-IF-OLD
    END-READ
END-IF

(This prevents "READ AFTER EOF" On systems that raise that, rather than just 
continuing to return AT END on subsequent READs...)

   READ TXFRHST-FILE NEXT RECORD
       NOT AT END
                PERFORM 0510-DELETE-IF-OLD
     END-IF


And yes, I do realize you
> can have END-PURGE defined as the 88 level name for the FILE STATUS
> for the file and do this instead.
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I think you may have gone a bit off the rails here, Frank... :-)

> Anyway, just felt like starting a new argument.  I have no
> expectation that this would ever become part of any COBOL standard.
> Just wasting time on a Friday evening...

A mind is such a terrible thing to waste... :-)

Pete.
```

## Thread: OT - Perl — 7 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT - Perl

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-18T12:08:31-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<m9qpg69jtd1vdkss0t7svqclocj53vk4c4@4ax.com>`

```

On this day (December 18) the first version of PERL was released in
1987.  Written by Larry Wall it combines syntax from several Unix
utilities and languages. Perl is designed to handle a variety of
system administrator functions and provides comprehensive string
handling functions.

Of course it has gone on to be much more than what it was originally
written for.  I don't know PERL but may try to learn it in 2011. Makes
for an interesting New Years Resolution.  

That it still survives today when many soup du jour languages have
fallen off the cliff, says a lot about it.  As does the fact that
COBOL, which is nearly 30 years older than PERL.

Regards,
```

#### ↳ Re: OT - Perl

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-18T12:49:58-08:00

```
On Dec 19, 6:08 am, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
> On this day (December 18) the first version of PERL was released in
> 1987.  Written by Larry Wall it combines syntax from several Unix
> utilities and languages. Perl is designed to handle a variety of
> system administrator functions and provides comprehensive string
> handling functions.

Practical Extraction and Reporting Language.

It started as a mix of awk, sed and bash. The main problem with it is
that everything can be done in several different ways. This means that
someone who can write PERL in one style may not be able to understand
programs written in a different style. The standard stype is 'write-
only'.

It also does not have OO, just a kludge that sort-of makes it like OO.


> Of course it has gone on to be much more than what it was originally
> written for.  I don't know PERL but may try to learn it in 2011. Makes
> for an interesting New Years Resolution.  

You probably should move to more modern OO scripting languages, like
many PERL programmers are: Python, Ruby, Javascript or several others.

> That it still survives today when many soup du jour languages have
> fallen off the cliff, says a lot about it.  As does the fact that
> COBOL, which is nearly 30 years older than PERL.

PERL is falling off the cliff. While still there in large quantities
it is being replaced.

http://langpop.com/
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT - Perl

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-26T11:36:34-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5ddb33e6-7cd6-4f8b-bca3-d5179c3359e9@n2g2000pre.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<97e7fab3-b071-4c84-8066-a7744a8504f5@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 26, 2:59 pm, chuck <charles.levi...@gmail.com> wrote:
> I'd second this.  Rather than Perl you could consider the newer scripting-cum-programming languages.  My own preference is for Python which has the curious characteristic of forcing you to indent conditional logic.

It is necessary to ensure that your editor either always use tab
characters or never uses them. A mixture can screw it up. The
advantage of the system is that you see the logic by indent exactly as
the compiler/runtime does (it does JIT).

> I haven't used it for OO programming, mostly scripting to automate admin type tasks but understand that if anything it's even more OO than Java (whatever that means :-)
> It's got a nice GUI design counterpart too in TkInter.

I prefer to have the UI separate from the code so I have used Glade.
The designer writes the UI to an XML file and the program basically
says 'show the UI' and does an autoconnect of the events in the UI to
functions in the program.

This runs on Linux, Windows and even my pocket Nokia N800 with no
source code changes.
```

<a id="t-c13321db76"></a>
## Thread: OT: Season's Greetings — 7 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: Season's Greetings

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-20T11:57:43+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8n7gv9Fn3fU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
As we enter the home straight for Xmas/New Year, I'd just like to take this 
opportunity of wishng everyone here a great festive season and a fruitful 
and happy New Year.

The local paper here asked me for a small piece to run on January 1st so I 
thought I'd share my effort with you. Consider it another worthless stocking 
filler :-)

NEW YEAR 2011
================
January.

Named after the Roman God, Janus; God of doors and new beginnings.

Janus is depicted as having two faces, one coming, one going.

I'm a bit like that with New Year Resolutions. Part of me knows at the time 
I make them that there is a very good chance I won't get to keep them, but 
the pragmatic side of my nature says: "Hey, if you even manage to do this 
for a few weeks, it will be a Good Thing."

So, I avoid the pastry counter, bite my tongue when there is an acerbic 
remark on it, suffer fools gladly or leave the room, am kind and sweet to 
relatives who make me want to scream,  politely decline when telemarketing 
people phone me at dinner time, and amass hundreds (maybe even thousands.) 
of Karmic Brownie points. for a few weeks.

And it IS a Good Thing.

For everyone except me.

My stress levels increase, I have unaccountable sweats, I start grinding my 
teeth in my fitful sleep, and my normally cheerful disposition becomes one 
of suppressed anger and tension and the certain knowledge that Doom is 
impending and there will be tears before bedtime.

I'm just not designed to be all things to all people.

Most of us are not.

It violates the natural condition for us to be what we are not in the 
interests of turning over a new leaf because it is a New Year.  Why should 
some arbitrary calendar date trigger a guilt-based rash of self flagellation 
and resolving to be better? Why don't we resolve to be better all through 
the year, whenever we realise there is some room for improvement?

When the tiny voice of conscience says: "Well, you could have done that 
better. I mean. telling her you don't want to see her any more because every 
time she opens her mouth it is to torture and kill the English Language, and 
you can't have a relationship with someone whose only adjective is "awesome" 
or something still considered obscene in the eyes of the Law, with a 
superlative being both of her two adjectives used together. " .

Perhaps THAT is the time to resolve to be gentler, rather than leaving it 
'til New Year.

Anyway, I wish you all luck with your New Year Resolutions.

This year I have resolved not to make any. (Except this one, of course.)

I think it is an achievable goal. It will be nice to succeed with 
Resolutions for a change.

<a id="t-c978cedb85"></a>
## Thread: Nested "Occurs Depending On" — 7 this month / 39 total

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### Nested "Occurs Depending On"

- **From:** Pakku <pakku@soccermail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-23T11:30:45-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6c2b59b6-6235-46e9-87c5-6c54dc46f3db@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com>`

```
Why does MVS(zOS) Cobol not allow a nested OCCURS to be a DEPENDING ON?  

Is this some compiler limitation or machine limitation?  Or just an arbitrary decision?
```

#### ↳ Re: Nested "Occurs Depending On"

- **From:** Richard Luketich <rluketich@rlyxx.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-23T19:06:19-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<oor7h6dn50b18mk07k1bscl8ufb50ofiok@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<6c2b59b6-6235-46e9-87c5-6c54dc46f3db@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com>`

```
Last I checked, it does. However, it does have some rules around the
ODO, or you may have a compiler option set that is disallowing the use
of nested OCCURS DEPENDING ON. Due to the amount of housekeeping and
the fact that I haven't come across an application that truly requires
such a data structure, I've never used it. (Although, I could see it
being very useful when processing an XML file.)

The Enterprise COBOL for z/OS LRM
(http://publibfp.boulder.ibm.com/epubs/pdf/igy3lr50.pdf) even mentions
this explicitely:

Certain uses of the OCCURS DEPENDING ON clause result in complex
OCCURS DEPENDING ON (ODO) items. The following constitute complex ODO
items:
- A data item described with an OCCURS DEPENDING ON clause that is
ollowed by a nonsubordinate elementary data item, described with or
without an OCCURS clause
- A data item described with an OCCURS DEPENDING ON clause that is
followed by a nonsubordinate group item
****************
- A group item that contains one or more subordinate items described
with an OCCURS DEPENDING ON clause
****************
- A data item described with an OCCURS clause or an OCCURS DEPENDING
ON clause that contains a subordinate data item described with an
OCCURS DEPENDING ON clause (a table that contains variable-length
elements)
- An index-name associated with a table that contains variable-length
elements



On Thu, 23 Dec 2010 11:30:45 -0800 (PST), Pakku <pakku@soccermail.com>
wrote:

>Why does MVS(zOS) Cobol not allow a nested OCCURS to be a DEPENDING ON?  
>
>Is this some compiler limitation or machine limitation?  Or just an arbitrary decision?
```

#### ↳ Re: Nested "Occurs Depending On"

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-26T19:05:24-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xu2dne7cMfvJe4rQnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<6c2b59b6-6235-46e9-87c5-6c54dc46f3db@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com>`

```
Pakku wrote:
> Why does MVS(zOS) Cobol not allow a nested OCCURS to be a DEPENDING
> ON?
>
> Is this some compiler limitation or machine limitation?  Or just an
> arbitrary decision?

It's quite logical. And common.

Imagine
01  Mydata.
   02  Mydata-really OCCURS 1 TO 100 DEPENDING ON My-Num  PIC X.
   02  Wally  PIC X.

So, where's Wally in memory?

At byte-101 in Mydata? Or somewhere between 2 and 101 depending on My-Num?

If the latter, does Wally get moved in the background every time My-Num 
changes?
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Nested "Occurs Depending On"

- **From:** Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@googlemail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-27T04:27:17-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7b6fe044-c77f-4aa5-934c-798ca071f627@g27g2000vbl.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 27, 1:05 am, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
> Pakku wrote:

…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> changes?

another order of complexity.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Nested "Occurs Depending On"

- **From:** Richard Luketich <rluketich@rlyxx.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-28T22:55:01-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<fiflh6t8dnnu5qrpn7kpo2eoa27uv66ep3@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<6c2b59b6-6235-46e9-87c5-6c54dc46f3db@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com> <xu2dne7cMfvJe4rQnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
On Sun, 26 Dec 2010 19:05:24 -0600, "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
wrote:

>Pakku wrote:
>> Why does MVS(zOS) Cobol not allow a nested OCCURS to be a DEPENDING
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>

In zOS COBOL, Wally is at the location immediately following
Mydata-really(My-Num). My recollection is that Wally is NOT moved
automatically when you increase My-Num, which is where using nested or
multiple ODO in the same structure becomes complex.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Nested "Occurs Depending On"

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-29T02:05:50-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<GZBSo.61639$Mg5.39410@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<6c2b59b6-6235-46e9-87c5-6c54dc46f3db@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com> <xu2dne7cMfvJe4rQnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fiflh6t8dnnu5qrpn7kpo2eoa27uv66ep3@4ax.com>`

```
"Richard Luketich" <rluketich@rlyxx.com> wrote in message 
news:fiflh6t8dnnu5qrpn7kpo2eoa27uv66ep3@4ax.com...
> On Sun, 26 Dec 2010 19:05:24 -0600, "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
> wrote:
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> multiple ODO in the same structure becomes complex.

Just a little clarification, in IBM mainframe COBOL (Enterprise COBOL and 
all compilers that I know of in the past),

The compiler/run-time LOOKS for WALLY at the "address" jsut after the 
CURRENT size of Mydata-erally (my-num).  This is used for both receiving ans 
ending references to WALLY, i..e it looks at that address to update or send 
from the field.

The DATA at that location is never implicitly moved.  So that when/if my-num 
changes, then the compiler/run-time will look at the "new" address for data 
(either to use as a sending or receivinhg item).  For some examples of how 
this wokrs (and man NOT be what is intended, check out the Micro Focus 
ODOSLIDE compiler directive).  OTOH, when Micro Focus uses NO-ODOSLIDE, then 
the MAXIUM size of the ODO fields is unsed and "WALLY" is always found at 
the same place (and with the expected content) - but that means that the 
actual "phsucial" size of the group MYDATA never chenges.

Either approah may well lead to "unexpected results" - but both are 
supported by their respective compiler.
```

#### ↳ Re: Nested "Occurs Depending On"

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2010-12-27T21:52:48-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jadSo.99196$UC6.85137@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<6c2b59b6-6235-46e9-87c5-6c54dc46f3db@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com>`

```
IBM Enterprise COBOL very definitely DOES allow for nested OCCURS DEPENDING 
on statements.  This is a (well documented) IBM extension.  Please provide 
the sample code and what compiler message you get.

This is EXPLICITLY disallowed by the '85 (and later) ANSI/ISO Standards.

Many "workstation" compilers also allow this as an extension.  See (for 
example) the Micro Focus "ODOSLIDE compiler directive.


"Pakku" <pakku@soccermail.com> wrote in message 
news:6c2b59b6-6235-46e9-87c5-6c54dc46f3db@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com...
> Why does MVS(zOS) Cobol not allow a nested OCCURS to be a DEPENDING ON?
>
> Is this some compiler limitation or machine limitation?  Or just an 
> arbitrary decision?
>
```

Unfortunately it only allows you entry into fake Germany. This was a country 
set up by the Americans during the cold war to act as a decoy in the event 
of the Eastern Bloc rolling tanks into the real Germany. The idea was that 
NATO forces could regroup in the real Germany while the fake one was being 
occupied.

With the collapse of the Iron Curtain, fake Germany was no longer necessary 
and I believe it has now been absorbed into Poland, the Czech Republic and 
some of Transylvania.

You need a fake German passport to gain entry and there are some amazing 
fake castles and fake hotels where you can stay. The fake beer is not bad 
(although not a patch on the real thing) and some of the fake theatres do 
some really excellent fake productions. I had some fake sauerbraten but it 
was made from carousel horses and tasted a bit wooden.

Overall, the exeperience of visiting could be described as "unreal" or even 
"fake"...

Pete.

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> Hi Pete,
>
> I presume you are ok .... after the recent aftershock? and not the
> hangover.

Hi Alistair,

yes, I'm fine thanks. I live in the Bay of Plenty, in the North Island and, 
although the North Island has also been subject to some major earthquakes (I 
still have some cracks in my concrete drive from one that happened here 
about 15 years ago), the serious disaster of recent times has been the huge 
earthquake which hit Christchurch, a major city in the South Island (pop. 
300,000) a couple of months ago. There have been several "aftershocks" (some 
that would qualify as earthquakes in their own right) and a large one 
yesterday (4.9) which damaged buildings in the city. Miraculously, nobody 
has died but the emotional scars are huge and the damage is estimated to 
around $3 billion.

It is simply a heartbreak for the people who live there; as fast as they 
rebuild or try to, another shock strikes.

New Zealand is having a hard time at the moment with 29 miners being killed 
in a coal mine explosion and the continuing tremors in Christchurch. 
Everyone is hopeful the New Year will be better...

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Aftershock

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-30T02:15:13+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8o0qj3Fi6nU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<a99836f1-f94b-4d8f-81de-2cb4375031d0@l7g2000vbv.googlegroups.com> <8nqll4FimgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Alistair Maclean wrote:
>> Hi Pete,
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

I may have spoken too soon... In the last few days, The Bay of Plenty has 
seen a number of earthquakes. As they were mainly sited off-shore and in the 
morning, I slept through them :-) however there was one today (Wednesday 
29th December) that was in the afternoon. I was away visiting friends up in 
the hills around Paeroa (about 50 miles away) so I never felt it, but some 
friends had felt it and mentioned it when I got home.

Here is a local newsclip:

<quote>
A second earthquake with a Richter magnitude of 3.3 was felt in parts of the 
Bay of Plenty Wednesday afternoon.

The quake, recorded by GNS Science, struck 10km north of Matata at a depth 
of 5km at 2.14pm, and could be felt in Whakatane.

"It is usual to have small swarms like this, it doesn't happen all the time, 
but its part of living in active New Zealand," says GNS Science duty 
seismologist Caroline Ashenden.

"There's no real trend to them. The earthquakes this week have been small 
and they could go on for a while or they could be over now, you can't pick 
it," says Caroline.

More than 2000 earthquakes have been reported by GNS Science in 2010 in the 
Bay of Plenty region, which includes Tauranga to the west, Opotiki to the 
east, south to Rotorua and north to the sea, including White Island.

A 4.4 magnitude earthquake was recorded at 8.57am Wednesday 10km north of 
Matata, at a depth of 7km, and a 5.4 magnitude earthquake was recorded 30km 
north-east of Tauranga on Monday, December 27 at 3am.
</quote>

As you can see, we are faily inured to them and, for the most part, there is 
no damage or harm to people or property. However,  the fact that there was a 
5.4 less than 20 miles from where I live is a bit disconcerting.

Still, nothing we can do about it, and I don't plan on emigrating :-)

It is part of the price we pay for living on a volcanic plateau. White 
island, which is mentioned in the news item above, is one of the weirdest 
places I have ever been.  I went out by helicopter and it affords a unique 
opportunity for photography of the crater of the active volcano. We landed 
in the crater, with breathing apparatus (the sulphur is pungent) and it was 
just astounding. Boiling mud, a red hot stream of fizzing water, beautiful 
configurations of discoloured clays and pure sulphur crystals sparkling in 
the sun, and clouds of ash puffing skywards from rock fumaroles that would 
take the skin off your hands if you touched them. Only about a quarter of 
the crater floor is "active" at present so there's a fairly large area to 
walk around on, but there are carefully marked paths and nobody would be 
inclined to ignore them. They tried a commercial sulphur mining operation in 
the 1930s and set up a plant and huts for the workmen. In the middle of the 
night there was an eruption of boiling mud and a lahar swept 11 men to a 
pretty horrible death. (Some of the bodies were never recovered being 
literally dissolved in the boiling flood). No attempt has been made to mine 
it since and today it is strictly for tourists, who spend about 90 minutes 
and visits are subject to a resident geologist in Whakatane giving the OK. 
There is a place where the molten sulphur runs as a stream into the Pacific 
Ocean and it is really spectacular. From the air you can see the yellow 
turning to green amidst huge of steam as the hot sulphur hits the sea.. The 
15 minute helicopter flight is about two and a half hours by boat, but the 
trip is good. We were fortunate enough to see a pod of dolphin frolicing in 
the Bay and apparently Orcas are fairly frequent.

http://www.vulcanheli.co.nz/

If you look at the "News" tab on this web site you will see that alerts 
regarding the island, quite often refer to associated earthquakes nearby. 
Some people believe that White island is like a pressure cooker valve and 
releases pressure from the plateau; if it wasn't there, a large part of the 
North Island would probably blow itself off the map. Lake Taupo, in the 
middle of the North Island was formed by a single volcanic explosion around 
25,000 years ago (not SO long in Geological terms...). The lake has an area 
of  238 square miles (616 sq. KM). There were subsequent sporadic eruptions 
until "the BIG one" in around 100 BC. This was one of the most violent 
eruptions on Planet Earth in the last 5,000 years.  The eruption column was 
twice as high as the eruption column from Mount St. Helens in 1980, and the 
ash turned the sky red over Rome and China. The eruption devastated much of 
the North Island and further expanded the lake.

It may be shaky, but it's "home" to me :-)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Printing on the client-side

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-12-30T11:05:16-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<eec0f226-5862-449d-8cef-330dd1169c03@f21g2000prn.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<ca26d265-1a5f-429b-a7c9-d05ebf46599e@p7g2000prb.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 30, 10:41 pm, iNFO_rene <infodynamics...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> Got a SAMPLE.txt file (Cobol line sequential) in the web server, say
> the text file resides in;
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Cobol V3.10

It is likely that you would need to CALL something to fetch the file
onto your local disk and then open and read it.

For example you could use wget or snarf:

       CALL "SYSTEM" USING "snarf http://www.myURLsite.com/reports/SAMPLE.txt
SAMPLE.txt"

Or you could write a simple Python script such as:

import httplib
conn = httplib.HTTPConnection("www.myURLsite.com")
conn.request("GET", "/reports/SAMPLE.txt")
r1 = conn.getresponse()
data1 = r1.read()
conn.close()
```

```
The objective is;

a. get or download the .TXT file on the webserver (coming from a URL
link)
b. write or duplicate the .TXT on the client-side (maybe line by line
as it is a sequential file)
c. print the .TXT file (as is)

All steps (a,b and c) written in MFocus Cobol. It is suppose to be
simple BUT I haven't seen Cobol application do this.

The usual browser-based application I did is to read part of the ASP/
HTML page (for reporting) and print it using an ActiveX (repackage
VBasic dll) printing module line by line. BUT this "only" works in IE
and I hate that. Most people now are using Firefox and Chrome browsers.
